- [Stokes' theorem](../../../../general/Stokes'%20theorem.md) ::@:: The [line integral](../../../../general/line%20integral.md) of a vector field over a loop is equal to the [surface integral](../../../../general/surface%20integral.md) of its _[curl](../../../../general/curl%20(mathematics).md)_ over the enclosed surface. <!--SR:!2025-08-18,130,408!2025-09-02,141,407-->
  - Stokes' theorem / mathematical statement ::@:: Let $\Sigma$ be a smooth oriented surface in $\mathbb {R} ^{3}$ with boundary $\partial \Sigma \equiv \Gamma$. If a vector field $\mathbf {F} (x,y,z)=(F_{x}(x,y,z),F_{y}(x,y,z),F_{z}(x,y,z))$ is defined and has continuous first order [partial derivatives](../../../../general/partial%20derivatives.md) in a region containing $\Sigma$, then $$\iint _{\Sigma }(\nabla \times \mathbf {F} )\cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {\Sigma } =\oint _{\partial \Sigma }\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {\Gamma } .$$ More explicitly, the equality says that $${\scriptstyle {\begin{aligned}&\iint _{\Sigma }\left(\left({\frac {\partial F_{z} }{\partial y} }-{\frac {\partial F_{y} }{\partial z} }\right)\,\mathrm {d} y\,\mathrm {d} z+\left({\frac {\partial F_{x} }{\partial z} }-{\frac {\partial F_{z} }{\partial x} }\right)\,\mathrm {d} z\,\mathrm {d} x+\left({\frac {\partial F_{y} }{\partial x} }-{\frac {\partial F_{x} }{\partial y} }\right)\,\mathrm {d} x\,\mathrm {d} y\right)\\&=\oint _{\partial \Sigma }{\Bigl (}F_{x}\,\mathrm {d} x+F_{y}\,\mathrm {d} y+F_{z}\,\mathrm {d} z{\Bigr )}.\end{aligned} } }$$ <!--SR:!2025-08-31,141,408!2026-05-25,333,388-->
  - Stokes' theorem / interpretation ::@:: Recall that curl measures the "twist" of vectors in a vector field. If you add all the "twist" in a surface, it equals the "twist" obtained by going around the boundary. <!--SR:!2025-08-14,126,408!2025-08-17,129,408-->
  - Stokes' theorem / note ::@:: Given a closed curve, you can choose any surface satisfying the conditions with the closed curve as a boundary. It does not affect the result. <p> For a closed surface (enclosing a volume), there is no boundary. In that case, the total "twist" is 0. <!--SR:!2025-08-27,137,408!2025-08-18,130,408-->

## week 5 lecture

- datetime: 2025-03-03T09:00:00+08:00/2025-03-03T10:20:00+08:00
- topic: electric conduction and currents
- [magnetostatics](../../../../general/magnetostatics.md) ::@:: It is the study of magnetic fields in systems where the currents are steady (not changing with time). It is the magnetic analogue of electrostatics, where the charges are stationary. <!--SR:!2025-08-21,132,408!2025-08-25,136,408-->
  - magnetostatics / vs. electrostatics ::@:: There are many similarities. The main difference is that while there are free electric monopoles \(free electric charges\), there are no free magnetic monopoles. <p> Thus it does not make sense to have a test magnetic monopole. Rather, we have test magnetic dipoles of magnetic moment __m__, and we calculate its magnetic moment ___τ___ \(see below\). <!--SR:!2025-08-12,124,408!2025-08-26,136,408-->
- [magnetic moment](../../../../general/magnetic%20moment.md) ::@:: It is the combination of strength and orientation of a magnet or other object or system that exerts a magnetic field. The magnetic dipole moment of an object determines the magnitude of torque the object experiences in a given magnetic field. <!--SR:!2025-08-25,136,408!2025-08-25,136,408-->
  - magnetic moment / definition ::@:: The relationship is given by: $${\boldsymbol {\tau } }=\mathbf {m} \times \mathbf {B}$$ where ___τ___ is the torque acting on the dipole, __B__ is the external magnetic field, and __m__ is the magnetic moment.  <p> More concretely, it can be defined as: $$\mathbf m(\mathbf r) = \int_V \! (\mathbf r' - \mathbf r) \times \mathbf J(\mathbf r') \,\mathrm d^3 \mathbf r' \,,$$ where $\mathbf r$ is a reference point. To derive this, consider the force per volume is $\mathbf J \times \mathbf B$, and thus the torque per volume is $(\mathbf r' - \mathbf r) \times \mathbf J \times \mathbf B$. Then we see $(\mathbf r' - \mathbf r) \times \mathbf J$ gives the magnetic moment per volume. <p> For a magnetic dipole, the above expression gives the same result no matter what $\mathbf r$ is. So we can just remove the argument $\mathbf r$ for magnetic dipoles \(which is always applicable unless you consider magnetic monopoles existing\). <!--SR:!2026-06-03,363,408!2025-07-02,89,388-->
    - magnetic moment / definition / analogy ::@:: For electric dipole moment, it can be defined as: $$\mathbf p(\mathbf r) = \int_V \! (\mathbf r' - \mathbf r) \rho(\mathbf r') \,\mathrm d^3 \mathbf r' \,,$$ where $\mathbf r$ is a reference point. (Whether $\rho$ includes _bound_ charges depends on the calculation context.) <p> Similarly, for magnetic moment, it can be defined as: $$\mathbf m(\mathbf r) = \int_V \! (\mathbf r' - \mathbf r) \times \mathbf J(\mathbf r') \,\mathrm d^3 \mathbf r' \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-07-14,46,373!2025-08-30,114,413-->
- [electric current](../../../../general/electric%20current.md) ::@:: It is a flow of charged particles, such as electrons or ions, moving through an electrical conductor or space. It is defined as the net rate of flow of electric charge through a surface. <!--SR:!2025-08-22,133,408!2025-08-29,139,408-->
  - electric current / SI unit ::@:: ampere \(A\) <!--SR:!2025-08-27,137,408!2025-08-28,138,408-->
  - electric current / experiment: electric current and conductivity of ions ::@:: We cannot conduct electricity through deionized \(DI\) water. But we can do so after adding sodium chloride \(NaCl\) to the DI water, which creates ions. <!--SR:!2025-08-25,136,408!2025-08-28,138,408-->
- [current density](../../../../general/current%20density.md) ::@:: It is the amount of charge per unit time that flows through a unit area of a chosen cross section. <!--SR:!2025-07-16,96,388!2025-08-13,125,408-->
  - current density / drift velocity ::@:: The velocity the charges drift at can be calculated from the equation: $$\mathbf J = n q \mathbf v \,,$$ where <p> - $n$ is number of charged particles per unit volume \(or charge carrier density\) <br/> - $\mathbf v$ is the [drift velocity](../../../../general/drift%20velocity.md), and <br/> - $q$ is the charge on each particle. <!--SR:!2025-09-08,142,420!2025-07-20,101,400-->
  - current density / to current through an area ::@:: The [surface integral](../../../../general/surface%20integral.md) of __j__ over a [surface](surface%20(mathematics).md) _S_ gives the total amount of current flowing through the surface: $$I=\iint _{S}\mathbf {j} \cdot \mathbf {\hat {n} } \,dA.$$ More concisely, this is the [flux](flux.md) of __j__ across _S_. <!--SR:!2025-08-18,130,408!2025-08-30,140,408-->
- [continuity equation](../../../../general/continuity%20equation.md) ::@:: It is an equation that describes the transport of some quantity. It is particularly simple and powerful when applied to a conserved quantity, but it can be generalized to apply to any extensive quantity. <!--SR:!2025-08-26,136,408!2025-08-30,140,408-->
  - continuity equation / vs. conservation laws ::@:: They are a stronger, local form of conservation laws. Conservation laws does not rule out the possibility that a quantity could disappear from one point while simultaneously appearing at another point. A stronger statement is that quantity is locally conserved: quantity can neither be created nor destroyed, nor can it "teleport" from one place to another—it can only move by a continuous flow. A continuity equation is the mathematical way to express this kind of statement. <!--SR:!2025-08-29,139,408!2025-08-16,128,408-->
  - continuity equation / integral form ::@:: Mathematically, the integral form of the continuity equation expressing the rate of increase of _q_ within a volume _V_ is: $${\frac {dq}{dt} }+\oint _{S}\mathbf {j} \cdot d\mathbf {S} =\Sigma$$ where <p> - _S_ is any imaginary [closed surface](../../../../general/closed%20surface.md#closed%20surfaces), that encloses a volume _V_, <br/> - $\oint _{S}d\mathbf {S}$ denotes a [surface integral](../../../../general/surface%20integral.md) over that closed surface, <br/> - _q_ is the total amount of the quantity in the volume _V_, <br/> - __j__ is the flux of _q_, <br/> - _t_ is time, <br/> - Σ is the net rate that _q_ is being generated inside the volume _V_ per unit time. <!--SR:!2025-08-24,135,408!2025-08-23,134,408-->
  - continuity equation / differential form ::@:: By the [divergence theorem](../../../../general/divergence%20theorem.md), a general continuity equation can also be written in a "differential form": $${\frac {\partial \rho }{\partial t} }+\nabla \cdot \mathbf {j} =\sigma$$ where <p> - ∇⋅ is [divergence](../../../../general/divergence.md), <br/> - _ρ_ is the density of the amount _q_ \(i.e. the quantity _q_ per unit volume\), <br/> - __j__ is the flux of _q_ \(i.e. __j__ = ρ<!-- markdown separator -->__v__, where __v__ is the vector field describing the movement of the quantity _q_\), <br/> - _t_ is time, <br/> - _σ_ is the generation of _q_ per unit volume per unit time. <!--SR:!2025-08-24,135,408!2025-08-25,136,408-->
  - continuity equation / electromagnetism ::@:: It states that the [divergence](../../../../general/divergence.md) of the [current density](../../../../general/current%20density.md) __J__ \(in [amperes](../../../../general/amperes.md) per square meter\) is equal to the negative rate of change of the [charge density](../../../../general/charge%20density.md) _ρ_ \(in [coulombs](../../../../general/coulomb.md) per cubic meter\), $$\nabla \cdot \mathbf {J} =-{\frac {\partial \rho }{\partial t} }$$ <!--SR:!2025-07-15,96,388!2025-08-27,137,408-->
- electric current
  - electric current / drift speed ::@:: The speed they drift at can be calculated from the equation: $$I=nAvQ\,,$$ where <p> - $I$ is the electric current <br/> - $n$ is number of charged particles per unit volume \(or charge carrier density\) <br/> - $A$ is the cross-sectional area of the conductor <br/> - $v$ is the [drift velocity](../../../../general/drift%20velocity.md), and <br/> - $Q$ is the charge on each particle. <!--SR:!2025-08-15,127,408!2026-02-05,252,388-->
- continuity equation
  - continuity equation / electromagnetism
    - continuity equation / electromagnetism / stationary ::@:: When $\frac {\partial \rho} {\partial t} = 0$, this is the stationary case. Then we can derive that current remains the same across any cross section of a wire and Kirchhoff's junction rule. <!--SR:!2025-08-30,140,408!2026-05-31,340,388-->
- [Kirchhoff's circuit laws](../../../../general/Kirchhoff's%20circuit%20laws.md) ::@:: They are two equalities that deal with the current and potential difference (commonly known as voltage) in the lumped element model of electrical circuits. They were first described in 1845 by German physicist Gustav Kirchhoff. <!--SR:!2025-08-14,126,408!2025-08-31,141,408-->
  - Kirchhoff's junction rule ::@:: Recalling that current is a signed \(positive or negative\) quantity reflecting direction towards or away from a node, this principle can be succinctly stated as: $$\sum _{i=1}^{n}I_{i}=0$$ where _n_ is the total number of branches with currents flowing towards or away from the node. <!--SR:!2025-08-25,136,408!2025-08-25,136,408-->
- [Ohm's law](Ohm's%20law.md) ::@:: It states that the [electric current](../../../../general/electric%20current.md) through a [conductor](../../../../general/electrical%20conductor.md) between two [points](../../../../general/node%20(circuits).md) is directly [proportional](../../../../general/proportionality%20(mathematics).md) to the [voltage](voltage.md) across the two points. <!--SR:!2025-08-15,127,408!2025-09-02,141,407-->
  - Ohm's law / equations ::@:: Introducing the constant of proportionality, the [resistance](../../../../general/electrical%20resistance.md), one arrives at the three mathematical equations used to describe this relationship: $$V=IR\quad {\text{or} }\quad I={\frac {V}{R} }\quad {\text{or} }\quad R={\frac {V}{I} }$$ where _I_ is the current through the conductor, _V_ is the voltage measured across the conductor and _R_ is the [resistance](../../../../general/electrical%20resistance.md) of the conductor. <!--SR:!2025-08-17,129,408!2025-08-18,130,408-->
  - Ohm's law / validity ::@:: It is an [empirical relation](../../../../general/empirical%20law.md) which accurately describes the conductivity of the vast majority of [electrically conductive materials](../../../../general/electrical%20conductor.md) over many orders of magnitude of current. However some materials do not obey Ohm's law; these are called [non-ohmic](../../../../general/non-ohmic%20resistance.md#static%20and%20differential%20resistance). <!--SR:!2025-08-24,135,408!2025-08-25,136,408-->
    - Ohm's law / validity / examples ::@:: Ohmic resistors obey Ohm's law: typical metal wire at typical temperature, etc. <br/> non-Ohmic resistors do not obey Ohm's law: semiconductor diode \(positive current increases nonlinearly with positive voltage; negative current is very little even with increasing negative voltage\), etc. <!--SR:!2025-09-01,140,407!2025-08-17,129,408-->
  - Ohm's law / vector form ::@:: is also used to refer to various generalizations of the law; for example the [vector](../../../../general/vector%20(mathematics%20and%20physics).md) form of the law used in [electromagnetics](../../../../general/electromagnetics.md) and material science: $$\mathbf {J} =\sigma \mathbf {E} ,$$ where __J__ is the [current density](../../../../general/current%20density.md) at a given location in a resistive material, __E__ is the electric field at that location, and _σ_ \([sigma](../../../../general/sigma.md)\) is a material-dependent parameter called the [conductivity](../../../../general/electrical%20conductivity.md), defined as the [inverse](../../../../general/inverse%20function.md) of [resistivity](../../../../general/electrical%20resistivity%20and%20conductivity.md) _ρ_ \([rho](../../../../general/rho.md)\). <p> In this case, Ohm's law refers to the conductivity _σ_ being independent of the electric field __E__. <!--SR:!2025-08-15,127,408!2025-08-25,136,408-->
- [electrical resistivity and conductivity](../../../../general/electrical%20resistivity%20and%20conductivity.md) ::@:: It is a fundamental [specific property](../../../../general/specific%20property.md) of a material that measures its [electrical resistance](../../../../general/electrical%20resistance.md) or how strongly it resists [electric current](../../../../general/electric%20current.md). A low resistivity indicates a material that readily allows electric current. <!--SR:!2025-08-28,138,408!2025-08-31,141,408-->
  - electrical resistivity and conductivity / range ::@:: Elemental metals, e.g. aluminum and copper, typically are on the order of 10<sup>–8</sup> Ω⋅m. Insulating materials, e.g. glass, typically are on the order of 10<sup>14</sup> Ω⋅m. <!--SR:!2025-08-24,135,408!2025-09-02,141,407-->
  - electrical resistivity and conductivity / SI unit ::@:: ohm metre \(Ω⋅m\), siemens per metre \(S/m\) <!--SR:!2025-08-24,135,408!2025-08-28,138,408-->
  - electrical resistivity and conductivity / Pouillet's law ::@:: Assuming the electric field and electrical resistivity throughout a wire of length $\ell$ and area $A$ is constant. Then we have $$\lvert \mathbf E \rvert = \rho \lvert \mathbf J \rvert \implies \frac V \ell = \rho \frac I A \implies V = \rho \frac \ell A I \,,$$ where $$R = \rho \frac \ell A$$ can be identified as the _electrical resistance_ $R$. The latter equation is known as __Pouillet's law__. <!--SR:!2025-08-29,139,408!2025-08-25,136,408-->

## week 5 lecture 2

- datetime: 2025-03-05T09:00:00+08:00/2025-03-05T10:20:00+08:00
- topic: magnetic forces
- [electromotive force](../../../../general/electromotive%20force.md) \(emf\) ::@:: It is an [energy](../../../../general/energy.md) transfer to an [electric circuit](../../../../general/electric%20circuit.md) per unit of [electric charge](../../../../general/electric%20charge.md), measured in [volts](../../../../general/volt.md). <p> However, electromotive force itself is not a physical force. <!--SR:!2025-07-10,93,393!2025-08-24,131,413-->
  - electromotive force / motivation ::@:: Consider applying an electric field to a conductor. Charges inside would create a current temporarily, which is soon stopped by the build-up of charges at the ends, as the electric field inside the conductor is now zero. This is exactly same as the situation of an ideal conductor in an electric field. <p> For a current to last, the charges must be part of a _closed_ circuit. Additionally, something needs to _push_ it, thus we have electromotive force \(which is not actually a physical force\). <!--SR:!2025-09-09,145,413!2025-09-09,145,413-->
  - electromotive force / sources ::@:: Devices called electrical _[transducers](../../../../general/transducer.md)_ provide an emf by [converting](../../../../general/energy%20transformation.md) other forms of [energy](../../../../general/energy.md) into [electrical energy](../../../../general/electrical%20energy.md). Other types of electrical equipment also produce an emf, such as [batteries](../../../../general/battery%20(electricity).md), which convert [chemical energy](../../../../general/chemical%20energy.md), and [generators](../../../../general/electric%20generator.md), which convert [mechanical energy](../../../../general/mechanical%20energy.md). This energy conversion is achieved by [physical forces](../../../../general/force.md) applying [physical work](../../../../general/work%20(physics).md) on [electric charges](../../../../general/electric%20charge.md). <!--SR:!2025-08-30,137,413!2025-07-12,95,393-->
  - electromotive force / vs. potential difference ::@:: They are formally distinct concepts: <p> - Potential difference is a more general term that includes emf. <br/> - Emf is the cause of a potential difference. <br/> - In a circuit of a voltage source and a resistor, the sum of the source's applied voltage plus the ohmic voltage drop through the resistor is zero. But the resistor provides no emf, only the voltage source does: <br/> &emsp; - For a circuit using a battery source, the emf is due solely to the chemical forces in the battery. <br/> &emsp; - For a circuit using an electric generator, the emf is due solely to a time-varying magnetic forces within the generator. <br/> - Both a 1 volt emf and a 1 volt potential difference correspond to 1 joule per coulomb of charge. <!--SR:!2025-09-06,142,413!2025-08-29,136,413-->
- Kirchhoff's circuit laws
  - Kirchhoff's voltage law ::@:: Similarly to Kirchhoff's current law, the voltage law can be stated as: $$\sum _{i=1}^{n}V_{i}=0$$ Here, _n_ is the total number of voltages measured. <!--SR:!2025-09-04,140,413!2025-09-05,141,413-->
- electromotive force
  - electromotive force / circuit ::@:: In a circuit of a voltage source and a resistor, the sum of the source's applied voltage \(emf\) plus the ohmic voltage drop \(_not_ emf\) through the resistor is zero. <p> That is, when a charge moves through the source, it gains energy. When a charge moves through the resistor, it loses energy. When it finishes a cycle, it has the same energy as it started \(same potential\). <!--SR:!2025-07-10,93,393!2025-09-12,148,413-->
- [internal resistance](../../../../general/internal%20resistance.md) ::@:: In [electrical engineering](../../../../general/electrical%20engineering.md), a practical [electric power source](../../../../general/power%20supply.md) which is a [linear circuit](../../../../general/linear%20circuit.md) may, according to [Thévenin's theorem](../../../../general/Thévenin's%20theorem.md), be represented as an ideal [voltage source](../../../../general/voltage%20source.md) in [series](../../../../general/series%20and%20parallel%20circuits.md) with an [impedance](../../../../general/electrical%20impedance.md). This impedance is termed the __\(this\)__ of the source. <!--SR:!2025-09-09,145,413!2025-09-06,142,413-->
  - internal resistance / impedance ::@:: It is the opposition to [alternating current](../../../../general/alternating%20current.md) presented by the combined effect of [resistance](../../../../general/electrical%20resistance.md) and [reactance](../../../../general/electrical%20reactance.md) in a [circuit](../../../../general/electrical%20circuit.md). \(__this course__: for simplicity, we only consider resistance right now\) <!--SR:!2025-09-10,146,413!2025-09-07,143,413-->
  - internal resistance / electromotive force ::@:: As a result of internal resistance, the actual potential difference between the terminals of a practical battery is: $$V_{ab} = \varepsilon - Ir \,,$$ where $r$ is its internal resistance. Thus, with a resistor of resistance $R$, we have $\varepsilon - Ir - IR = 0$. <!--SR:!2025-08-29,136,413!2025-09-05,141,413-->
- [Lorentz force](../../../../general/Lorentz%20force.md) ::@:: The __Lorentz force law__ is the combination of electric and magnetic [force](../../../../general/force.md) on a [point charge](../../../../general/point%20charge.md#point%20charge) due to [electromagnetic fields](../../../../general/electromagnetic%20field.md). The __Lorentz force__, on the other hand, is a [physical effect](../../../../general/phenomenon.md) that occurs in the vicinity of electrically neutral, current-carrying conductors causing moving electrical charges to experience a __magnetic force__. <!--SR:!2025-08-27,134,413!2025-07-23,99,393-->
  - Lorentz force / experiment: magnetic force on a current carrying wire ::@:: A permanent magnet applies force on a current carrying wire. Reversing the current direction or the magnet orientation \(but not both at the same time\) reverses the force direction. <!--SR:!2025-09-07,143,413!2025-08-27,134,413-->
  - Lorentz force / experiment: magnetic force on an electric beam ::@:: A permanent magnet deflects an electron beam when the magnetic orientation is not parallel to the beam direction. Reversing the magnet orientation reverses the deflection direction. <!--SR:!2025-07-11,94,393!2025-09-08,144,413-->
  - Lorentz force / equation ::@:: It states that a particle of charge _q_ moving with a velocity __v__ in an [electric field](../../../../general/electric%20field.md) __E__ and a [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) __B__ experiences a force \(in [SI units](../../../../general/International%20System%20of%20Units.md) of $$\mathbf {F} =q\left(\mathbf {E} +\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} \right).$$ <!--SR:!2025-09-04,140,413!2026-06-04,343,393-->
    - Lorentz force / equation / interpretation ::@:: It says that the electromagnetic force on a charge _q_ is a combination of \(1\) a force in the direction of the electric field __E__ \(proportional to the magnitude of the field and the quantity of charge\), and \(2\) a force at right angles to both the magnetic field __B__ and the velocity __v__ of the charge \(proportional to the magnitude of the field, the charge, and the velocity\). <p> We also notice that the magnetic force does _not_ do work on electric charges, since it is always perpendicular to the velocity. <!--SR:!2025-07-21,97,393!2025-08-26,133,413-->
- [cyclotron motion](../../../../general/cyclotron%20motion.md) ::@:: It refers to the [circular motion](../../../../general/circular%20motion.md) exhibited by [charged particles](../../../../general/charged%20particle.md) in a uniform [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md). <!--SR:!2025-08-28,135,413!2025-08-28,135,413-->
  - cyclotron motion / Lorentz force ::@:: If a particle with electric charge $q$ and mass $m$ is moving with velocity ${\vec {v} }$ in a uniform magnetic field ${\vec {B} }$, then it will experience a [Lorentz force](../../../../general/Lorentz%20force.md) given by $${\vec {F} }=q({\vec {v} }\times {\vec {B} }).$$ The direction of the force is given by the [cross product](../../../../general/cross%20product.md) of the velocity and magnetic field. Thus, the Lorentz force will always act perpendicular to the direction of motion, causing the particle to [gyrate](../../../../general/gyration.md), or move in a circle. <!--SR:!2025-08-24,131,413!2025-09-10,146,413-->
  - cyclotron motion / cyclotron radius ::@:: The radius of this circle, $r_{\rm {c} }$, can be determined by equating the magnitude of the Lorentz force to the [centripetal force](../../../../general/centripetal%20force.md) as $${\frac {mv_{\perp }^{2} }{r_{\rm {c} } } }=|q|v_{\perp }B.$$ Rearranging, the cyclotron radius can be expressed as $$r_{\rm {c} }={\frac {mv_{\perp } }{|q|B} }.$$ Thus, the cyclotron radius is [directly proportional](../../../../general/directly%20proportional.md#direct%20proportionality) to the particle mass and perpendicular velocity, while it is inversely proportional to the particle electric charge and the magnetic field strength. <!--SR:!2026-01-16,232,373!2025-07-20,96,393-->
  - cyclotron motion / cyclotron frequency ::@:: The time it takes the particle to complete one revolution, called the [period](../../../../general/frequency.md#definitions%20and%20units), can be calculated to be $$T_{\rm {c} }={\frac {2\pi r_{\rm {c} } }{v_{\perp } } }.$$ The period is the [reciprocal](../../../../general/multiplicative%20inverse.md) of the cyclotron frequency: $$f_{\rm {c} }={\frac {1}{T_{\rm {c} } } }={\frac {|q|B}{2\pi m} }$$ or $$\omega _{\rm {c} }={\frac {|q|B}{m} }.$$ The cyclotron frequency is independent of the radius and velocity and therefore independent of the particle's kinetic energy; in the non-relativistic limit all particles with the same charge-to-mass ratio rotate around magnetic field lines with the same frequency. <!--SR:!2025-11-14,169,353!2026-07-12,382,393-->
  - cyclotron motion / parallel velocity ::@:: The velocity component parallel to the uniform magnetic field is unaffected. <!--SR:!2025-09-06,142,413!2025-08-25,132,413-->
- Lorentz force
  - Lorentz force / Laplace force ::@:: When a wire carrying an electric current is placed in a magnetic field, each of the moving charges, which comprise the current, experiences the Lorentz force, and together they can create a macroscopic force on the wire \(sometimes called the __Laplace force__\). <!--SR:!2025-08-27,134,413!2025-07-11,94,393-->
    - Lorentz force / Laplace force / equation ::@:: If, in addition, the magnetic field is inhomogeneous, the net force on a stationary rigid wire carrying a steady current _I_ is given by integration along the wire, $$\mathbf {F} =I\int \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {\ell } }\times \mathbf {B} .$$ \(This assumes the current is the same across the wire, which is typically the case.\) <!--SR:!2025-07-10,93,393!2026-01-15,231,373-->
    - Lorentz force / Laplace force / application ::@:: One application of this is [Ampère's force law](Ampère's%20force%20law.md), which describes how two current-carrying wires can attract or repel each other, since each experiences a Lorentz force from the other's magnetic field. <!--SR:!2025-08-26,133,413!2025-07-12,95,393-->

## week 5 tutorial

- datetime: 2025-03-06T18:00:00+08:00/2025-03-06T18:50:00+08:00
- topic: Drude model
- [Drude model](../../../../general/Drude%20model.md) ::@:: It of [electrical conduction](../../../../general/electrical%20conduction.md) was proposed in 1900 by [Paul Drude](../../../../general/Paul%20Karl%20Ludwig%20Drude.md) to explain the transport properties of [electrons](../../../../general/electron.md) in materials \(especially metals\). <!--SR:!2025-07-11,94,393!2025-09-08,144,413-->
  - Drude model / motivation ::@:: The Drude model attempts to explain the resistivity of a conductor in terms of the scattering of electrons \(the carriers of electricity\) by the relatively immobile ions in the metal that act like obstructions to the flow of electrons. <!--SR:!2025-08-30,137,413!2025-09-04,140,413-->
  - Drude model / description ::@:: The model, which is an application of [kinetic theory](../../../../general/kinetic%20theory%20of%20gases.md), assumes that the microscopic behaviour of electrons in a solid may be treated classically and behaves much like a [pinball](../../../../general/pinball.md) machine, with a sea of constantly jittering electrons bouncing and re-bouncing off heavier, relatively immobile positive ions. <!--SR:!2025-07-25,99,393!2025-07-17,100,393-->
  - Drude model / mathematical treatment
    - Drude model / mathematical treatment / DC field ::@:: The simplest analysis of the Drude model assumes that electric field __E__ is both uniform and constant, and that the thermal velocity of electrons is sufficiently high such that they accumulate only an infinitesimal amount of momentum _d_<!-- markdownlint MD028 -->__p__ between collisions, which occur on average every _τ_ seconds. <!--SR:!2025-08-24,131,413!2025-08-25,132,413-->
      - Drude model / mathematical treatment / DC field / derivation ::@:: Then an electron isolated at time _t_ will on average have been travelling for time _τ_ since its last collision, and consequently will have accumulated momentum $$\Delta \langle \mathbf {p} \rangle =q\mathbf {E} \tau .$$ During its last collision, this electron will have been just as likely to have bounced forward as backward, so all prior contributions to the electron's momentum may be ignored, resulting in the expression $$\langle \mathbf {p} \rangle =q\mathbf {E} \tau .$$ Substituting the relations $${\begin{aligned}\langle \mathbf {p} \rangle &=m\langle \mathbf {v} \rangle ,\\\mathbf {J} &=nq\langle \mathbf {v} \rangle ,\end{aligned} }$$ results in the formulation of Ohm's law mentioned above: $$\mathbf {J} =\left({\frac {nq^{2}\tau }{m} }\right)\mathbf {E} \,,$$ where $n$ is number of charged particles per unit volume \(or charge carrier density\), $q$ is the charge on each particle, and $m$ is the mass of each particle. <!--SR:!2025-09-10,125,333!2026-07-15,385,393-->
      - Drude model / mathematical treatment / DC field / conductivity ::@:: The material can be shown to satisfy Ohm's law $\mathbf {J} =\sigma _{0}\mathbf {E}$ with a [DC](../../../../general/direct%20current.md)-conductivity _σ_<sub>0</sub>: $$\sigma _{0}={\frac {nq^{2}\tau }{m} } \,,$$ where $n$ is number of charged particles per unit volume \(or charge carrier density\), $q$ is the charge on each particle, and $m$ is the mass of each particle. <p> We can see materials with more charge carriers \(higher $n$\), e.g. metals, are more conductive, as opposed to, e.g. insulators. <p> We also see materials with higher collision time $\tau$, e.g. _elemental_ metals, are more conductive, as opposed to, e.g. alloys, which have more disorder. <!--SR:!2026-07-13,383,393!2026-07-14,384,393-->
- [network analysis](../../../../general/network%20analysis%20(electrical%20circuits).md) ::@:: It is the process of finding the [voltages](../../../../general/voltage.md) across, and the [currents](../../../../general/electric%20current.md) through, all network components. There are many techniques for calculating these values; however, for the most part, the techniques assume [linear](../../../../general/linear%20circuit.md) components. Except where stated, the methods described in this article are applicable only to _linear_ network analysis. <!--SR:!2025-08-25,132,413!2025-09-08,144,413-->
  - network analysis / note ::@:: In this course, we consider resistance only instead of impedance for now when doing network analysis. <!--SR:!2025-09-04,140,413!2025-07-30,104,393-->
  - network analysis / series, parallel ::@:: Some two terminal network of impedances can eventually be reduced to a single impedance by successive applications of impedances in series or impedances in parallel. <p> - Impedances in [series](../../../../general/series%20and%20parallel%20circuits.md#series%20circuits): $$Z_{\mathrm {eq} }=Z_{1}+Z_{2}+\,\cdots \,+Z_{n}.$$ <br/> - Impedances in [parallel](../../../../general/series%20and%20parallel%20circuits.md#parallel%20circuits): $${\frac {1}{Z_{\mathrm {eq} } } }={\frac {1}{Z_{1} } }+{\frac {1}{Z_{2} } }+\,\cdots \,+{\frac {1}{Z_{n} } }.$$ <!--SR:!2025-09-04,140,413!2025-09-03,139,413-->
  - network analysis / Kirchhoff's circuit laws ::@:: There are complicated circuits that cannot be reduced by using only series and parallel transformations. To analyze such circuits, we consider as a network of loops and junctions, and apply Kirchhoff's circuit laws on it. <p> \(The concrete steps involved are complex and is not listed here.\) <!--SR:!2025-09-10,146,413!2025-09-05,141,413-->
- [nodal analysis](../../../../general/nodal%20analysis.md) ::@:: It is a method of determining the voltage (potential difference) between "nodes" (points where elements or branches connect) in an electrical circuit in terms of the branch currents. <p> It is essentially a systematic application of Kirchhoff's current law (KCL) for circuit analysis. <!--SR:!2025-09-07,143,413!2025-08-26,133,413-->
- [mesh analysis](../../../../general/mesh%20analysis.md) ::@:: It is a circuit analysis method for planar circuits. Planar circuits are circuits that can be drawn on a plane surface with no wires crossing each other. A more general technique, called _loop analysis_ (with the corresponding network variables called _loop currents_) can be applied to any circuit, planar or not. <p> It makes systematic use of Kirchhoff’s voltage law to arrive at a set of equations guaranteed to be solvable if the circuit has a solution. <!--SR:!2025-09-03,139,413!2025-07-12,95,393-->
