## week 7 lecture

- datetime: 2025-03-17T09:00:00+08:00/2025-03-17T10:20:00+08:00
- topic: Biot–Savart law
- [Biot–Savart law](../../../../general/Biot–Savart%20law.md) ::@:: It is an equation describing the magnetic field generated by a constant electric current. It relates the magnetic field to the magnitude, direction, length, and proximity of the electric current. <!--SR:!2025-09-07,136,423!2025-09-25,148,423-->
  - Biot–Savart law / experiment ::@:: Applying current to an electric loop deflects a compass needle to a certain direction. Reversing the current reverses the deflection direction. <!--SR:!2025-07-31,103,403!2025-07-31,103,403-->
  - Biot–Savart law / equation ::@:: The equation in [SI](../../../../general/SI.md) units [teslas \(T\)](../../../../general/tesla%20(unit).md) is $$\mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} )={\frac {\mu _{0} }{4\pi } }\int _{C}{\frac {I\,d{\boldsymbol {\ell } }\times \mathbf {r'} }{|\mathbf {r'} |^{3} } }$$ where $d{\boldsymbol {\ell } }$ is a vector along the path $C$ whose magnitude is the length of the [differential](../../../../general/infinitesimal.md) element of the wire in the direction of _[conventional current](../../../../general/conventional%20current.md#conventions)_, ${\boldsymbol {\ell } }$ is a point on path $C$, and $\mathbf {r'} =\mathbf {r} -{\boldsymbol {\ell } }$ is the full [displacement vector](../../../../general/displacement%20vector.md) from the wire element \($d{\boldsymbol {\ell } }$\) at point ${\boldsymbol {\ell } }$ to the point at which the field is being computed \($\mathbf {r}$\), and _μ_<sub>0</sub> is the [magnetic constant](../../../../general/magnetic%20constant.md). <!--SR:!2025-07-06,79,383!2025-06-30,77,383-->
- [vacuum permeability](../../../../general/vacuum%20permeability.md) ::@:: It is the [magnetic permeability](../../../../general/magnetic%20permeability.md) in a [classical vacuum](../../../../general/classical%20vacuum.md#electromagnetism). It is a [physical constant](../../../../general/physical%20constant.md), conventionally written as ___μ_<sub>0</sub>__ \(pronounced "mu nought" or "mu zero"\). It quantifies the strength of the [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) induced by an [electric current](../../../../general/electric%20current.md). <!--SR:!2025-09-27,150,423!2025-09-25,148,423-->
  - vacuum permeability / SI units ::@:: Expressed in terms of [SI base units](../../../../general/SI%20base%20units.md), it has the unit kg⋅m⋅s<sup>−2</sup>⋅A<sup>−2</sup>. It can be also expressed in terms of [SI derived units](../../../../general/SI%20derived%20units.md), [N](../../../../general/newton%20(unit).md)⋅A<sup>−2</sup>. <!--SR:!2025-09-17,145,423!2025-09-28,151,423-->
  - vacuum permeability / CODATA ::@:: _μ_<sub>0</sub> = 1.25663706127\(20\)×10<sup>−6</sup> N⋅A<sup>−2</sup> <br/> \(lecture slides: _μ_<sub>0</sub> ≈ 4π×10<sup>−7</sup> N⋅A<sup>−2</sup>\) <!--SR:!2025-07-14,67,323!2025-07-10,82,383-->
  - vacuum permeability / relation with vacuum permittivity ::@:: In the [International System of Quantities](../../../../general/International%20System%20of%20Quantities.md) \(ISQ\), the [speed of light](../../../../general/speed%20of%20light.md) in vacuum, _c_, is related to the magnetic constant and the [electric constant \(vacuum permittivity\)](../../../../general/vacuum%20permittivity.md), _ε_<sub>0</sub>, by the equation: $$c^{2}={1 \over {\mu _{0}\varepsilon _{0} } }.$$ This relation can be derived using [Maxwell's equations](../../../../general/Maxwell's%20equations.md) of classical electromagnetism in the medium of [classical vacuum](../../../../general/vacuum.md#in%20electromagnetism). <!--SR:!2025-07-31,103,403!2025-09-15,143,423-->
- Biot–Savart law
  - Biot–Savart law / infinitely long wire ::@:: We can derive the magnetic field strength at a point due to a straight segment of a current-carrying wire in terms of the initial and final angles from the point to the wire. Then we have $$\frac {\mu_0 I} {4 \pi r} \left(\sin \theta_2 - \sin \theta_1 \right) \,,$$ where $r$ is the closest distance to the wire. <p> Consider $\theta_2 = \pi / 2$ and $\theta_1 = -\pi / 2$ for an _infinitely long wire_. Then we have $$\frac {\mu_0 I} {2 \pi r} \,.$$ We also see that this force has cylindrical symmetry. <!--SR:!2025-09-27,150,423!2025-09-18,146,423-->
- [Ampère's force law](../../../../general/Ampère's%20force%20law.md) ::@:: In [magnetostatics](../../../../general/magnetostatics.md), the force of attraction or repulsion between two [current](../../../../general/electric%20current.md)-carrying wires \(see first figure below\) is often called __\(this\)__. The physical origin of this force is that each wire generates a [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md), following the [Biot–Savart law](../../../../general/Biot–Savart%20law.md), and the other wire experiences a magnetic force as a consequence, following the [Lorentz force law](../../../../general/Lorentz%20force%20law.md). <!--SR:!2025-09-11,140,423!2025-09-09,138,423-->
  - Ampère's force law / equation ::@:: It states that the magnetic force per unit length between two straight parallel conductors is $${\frac {F_{m} }{L} }=2k_{\rm {A} }{\frac {I_{1}I_{2} }{r} },$$ \(annotation: note the denominator is $r$ instead of $r^2$\) where $k_{\rm {A} }$ is the magnetic force constant from the [Biot–Savart law](../../../../general/Biot–Savart%20law.md), $F_{m}/L$ is the total force on either wire per unit length of the shorter \(the longer is approximated as infinitely long relative to the shorter\), $r$ is the distance between the two wires, and $I_{1}$, $I_{2}$ are the [direct currents](../../../../general/direct%20current.md) carried by the wires. <!--SR:!2026-03-26,273,383!2025-09-26,149,423-->
    - Ampère's force law / equation / general case ::@:: For two arbitrary wires, using Biot–Savart law and then Lorentz force law, we can use iterated line integral and cross product to express the equation. It looks like this: $$\mathbf {F} _{12}={\frac {\mu _{0} }{4\pi } }\int _{L_{1} }\int _{L_{2} }{\frac {I_{1}d{\boldsymbol {\ell } }_{1}\ \times \ (I_{2}d{\boldsymbol {\ell } }_{2}\ \times \ {\hat {\mathbf {r} } }_{21})}{|r|^{2} } } \,.$$  where <p> - $\mathbf {F} _{12}$ is the total magnetic force felt by wire 1 due to wire 2 \(usually measured in [newtons](../../../../general/newton%20(unit).md)\), <br/> - $I_{1}$ and $I_{2}$ are the currents running through wires 1 and 2, respectively \(usually measured in [amperes](../../../../general/ampere.md)\), <br/> - The double line integration sums the force upon each element of wire 1 due to the magnetic field of each element of wire 2, <br/> - $d{\boldsymbol {\ell } }_{1}$ and $d{\boldsymbol {\ell } }_{2}$ are infinitesimal vectors associated with wire 1 and wire 2 respectively \(usually measured in [metres](../../../../general/metre.md)\); see [line integral](../../../../general/line%20integral.md) for a detailed definition, <br/> - The vector ${\hat {\mathbf {r} } }_{21}$ is the [unit vector](../../../../general/unit%20vector.md) pointing from the differential element on wire 2 towards the differential element on wire 1, and _\|r\|_ is the distance separating these elements, <br/> - The multiplication __×__ is a [vector cross product](../../../../general/cross%20product.md), <br/> - The sign of $I_{n}$ is relative to the orientation $d{\boldsymbol {\ell } }_{n}$ \(for example, if $d{\boldsymbol {\ell } }_{1}$ points in the direction of [conventional current](conventional%20current.md#conventions), then $I_{1}>0$\). <!--SR:!2025-10-07,131,343!2025-07-01,78,383-->
  - Ampère's force law / effect ::@:: Two wires attract each other if their current directions are the same. Two wires repel each other if their current directions are opposite. <!--SR:!2025-09-25,148,423!2025-09-11,140,423-->
- [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) ::@:: It is a physical field that describes the magnetic influence on moving electric charges, electric currents, and magnetic materials. <!--SR:!2025-09-24,147,423!2025-09-24,147,423-->
  - magnetic field / experiment ::@:: Iron fillings around a magnetic field source \(e.g. powered solenoids\) experience a torque that aligns them to the magnetic field lines. <!--SR:!2025-09-23,146,423!2025-09-27,150,423-->
  - magnetic field / interpretation ::@:: A moving charge in a magnetic field experiences a force perpendicular to its own velocity and to the magnetic field. <p> So what does the magnetic field direction represent? Recall that a magnetic dipole experiences a torque under a magnetic field: $\boldsymbol \tau = \mathbf m \times \mathbf B$. This torque is minimized if the dipole is parallel to the magnetic field direction. <!--SR:!2025-09-14,142,423!2025-09-22,145,423-->
  - magnetic field / visualization ::@:: Similar to electric field lines, it can be visualized using magnetic field lines. <p> The field lines start from the north poles and end at the south poles. The field line is always tangent to the magnetic field direction. The density of field lines indicates the magnetic field magnitude nearby \(denser is higher\). <!--SR:!2025-09-23,146,423!2025-09-27,150,423-->

## week 7 lecture 2

- datetime: 2025-03-19T09:00:00+08:00/2025-03-19T10:20:00+08:00
- topic: Maxwell equation of magnetostatics, magnetic potential
- [Ampère's circuital law](../../../../general/Ampère's%20circuital%20law.md) ::@:: It relates the circulation of a magnetic field around a closed loop to the electric current passing through the loop. <!--SR:!2025-09-26,149,423!2025-07-30,102,403-->
  - Ampère's circuital law / integral form ::@:: $$\oint _{C}\mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} {\boldsymbol {l} }=\mu _{0}\iint _{S}\mathbf {J} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {S} =\mu _{0}I_{\mathrm {enc} }$$ <p> Any surface $\mathbf S$ can be chosen. Use the right hand grip rule to determine the signs. <!--SR:!2025-08-05,108,403!2026-04-11,300,383-->
    - Ampère's circuital law / integral form / derivation ::@:: One can consider evaluating a closed loop \(not necessarily circular\) around a current-carrying wire. Then replace the current-carrying wire by current density going through the closed loop. <!--SR:!2025-09-26,149,423!2025-09-26,149,423-->
  - Ampère's circuital law / differential form ::@:: $$\mathbf {\nabla } \times \mathbf {B} =\mu _{0}\mathbf {J}$$ <!--SR:!2025-07-30,102,403!2025-08-16,108,403-->
    - Ampère's circuital law / differential form / derivation ::@:: Stokes' theorem can be used to derive this from the integral form of Ampère's circuital law. <!--SR:!2025-07-30,102,403!2025-09-23,146,423-->
  - Ampère's circuital law / analogy ::@:: This law is to Biot–Savart law like Gauss's law is to Coulomb's law. <!--SR:!2025-09-11,140,423!2025-09-24,147,423-->
  - Ampère's circuital law / application ::@:: We can use it to derive Biot–Savart law again. Or we can use it to derive the magnetic field inside a current-carrying wire with certain thickness \(for outside, it is the same as a wire of negligible thickness\). <!--SR:!2025-09-28,151,423!2025-09-22,145,423-->
- Biot–Savart law
  - Biot–Savart law / equation
    - Biot–Savart law / equation / current density ::@:: The proper formulation of the Biot–Savart law \(again in [SI](../../../../general/SI.md) units\) is: $$\mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} )={\frac {\mu _{0} }{4\pi } }\iiint _{V}\ {\frac {(\mathbf {J} \, \mathrm dV)\times \mathbf {r} '}{|\mathbf {r} '|^{3} } }$$ where $\mathbf {r'}$ is the vector from $\mathrm dV$ to the observation point $\mathbf {r}$, $\mathrm dV$ is the [volume element](../../../../general/volume%20element.md), and $\mathbf {J}$ is the [current density](../../../../general/current%20density.md) vector in that volume \(in SI in units of A/m<sup>2</sup>\). <!--SR:!2026-02-28,267,383!2026-02-27,266,383-->
    - Biot–Savart law / equation / curl ::@:: In a _magnetostatic_ situation, $$\begin{aligned} \mathbf {B} (\mathbf {r} ) & ={\frac {\mu _{0} }{4\pi } }\iiint _{V}\ {\frac {\left(\mathbf {J}(\mathbf \ell) \, \mathrm dV\right)\times (\mathbf r - \mathbf \ell)}{\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert^{3} } } \\ & = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \iiint_V \! \nabla_{\mathbf r} \left(\frac 1 {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \right) \times \mathbf J(\mathbf \ell) \,\mathrm dV \\ & = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \iiint_V \! \left( \nabla_{\mathbf r} \times \left(\frac {\mathbf J(\mathbf \ell)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \right) - \frac 1 {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \nabla_{\mathbf r} \times \mathbf J(\mathbf \ell) \right) \,\mathrm dV \\ & = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \nabla_{\mathbf r} \times \iiint_V \! \frac {\mathbf J(\mathbf \ell)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \,\mathrm dV \,. \end{aligned}$$ <p> We instantly see that the divergence of the magnetic field is zero. We obtain Gauss's law for magnetism. <p> Applying curl and further derivation yields Ampère's circuital law \(without Maxwell's correction\). In particular, it makes use of $\nabla \times (\nabla \times \mathbf r) = \nabla(\nabla \cdot \mathbf r) - \nabla^2 \mathbf r$ \(similar to $\mathbf a \times (\mathbf b \times \mathbf c) = (\mathbf a \cdot \mathbf c) \mathbf b - (\mathbf a \cdot \mathbf b) \mathbf c$\). <!--SR:!2026-01-16,205,363!2025-07-31,98,403-->
- [Gauss's law for magnetism](../../../../general/Gauss's%20law%20for%20magnetism.md) ::@:: It is one of the four [Maxwell's equations](../../../../general/Maxwell's%20equations.md) that underlie [classical electrodynamics](../../../../general/classical%20electrodynamics.md). It states that the [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) __B__ has [divergence](../../../../general/divergence.md) equal to zero, in other words, that it is a [solenoidal vector field](../../../../general/solenoidal%20vector%20field.md). It is equivalent to the statement that [magnetic monopoles](../../../../general/magnetic%20monopole.md) do not exist. Rather than "magnetic charges", the basic entity for magnetism is the [magnetic dipole](../../../../general/magnetic%20dipole.md#field%20from%20a%20magnetic%20dipole). <!--SR:!2025-09-08,137,423!2025-09-28,151,423-->
  - Gauss's law for magnetism / differential form ::@:: It is: $$\nabla \cdot \mathbf {B} =0$$ where ∇ · denotes [divergence](../../../../general/divergence.md), and __B__ is the [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md). <!--SR:!2025-09-15,143,423!2025-09-22,145,423-->
  - Gauss's law for magnetism / integral form ::@:: It states: $$\Phi _{B}= \oiint_S \mathbf {B} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {S} =0$$ where _S_ is any [closed surface](../../../../general/closed%20surface.md#closed%20surfaces) \(see image right\), $\Phi _{B}$ is the magnetic flux through _S_, and d<!-- markdown separator -->__S__ is a [vector](../../../../general/vector%20(geometric).md), whose magnitude is the area of an [infinitesimal](../../../../general/infinitesimal.md) piece of the surface _S_, and whose direction is the outward-pointing [surface normal](../../../../general/surface%20normal.md) \(see [surface integral](../../../../general/surface%20integral.md) for more details\). <!--SR:!2025-09-08,137,423!2025-07-30,102,403-->
- [vector potential](../../../../general/vector%20potential.md) ::@:: It is a [vector field](../../../../general/vector%20field.md) whose [curl](../../../../general/curl%20(mathematics).md) is a given vector field. This is analogous to a _[scalar potential](../../../../general/scalar%20potential.md)_, which is a scalar field whose [gradient](../../../../general/gradient.md) is a given vector field. <p> Formally, given a vector field $\mathbf {v}$, a _vector potential_ is a $C^{2}$ vector field $\mathbf {A}$ such that $$\mathbf {v} =\nabla \times \mathbf {A} .$$ <!--SR:!2025-07-29,102,403!2025-09-16,144,423-->
  - vector potential / consequence ::@:: If a vector field $\mathbf {v}$ admits a vector potential $\mathbf {A}$, then from the equality $$\nabla \cdot (\nabla \times \mathbf {A} )=0$$ \([divergence](../../../../general/divergence.md) of the [curl](../../../../general/curl%20(mathematics).md) is zero\) one obtains $$\nabla \cdot \mathbf {v} =\nabla \cdot (\nabla \times \mathbf {A} )=0,$$ which implies that $\mathbf {v}$ must be a [solenoidal vector field](../../../../general/solenoidal%20vector%20field.md). <!--SR:!2025-07-31,103,403!2025-09-17,145,423-->
  - vector potential / non-uniqueness ::@:: The vector potential admitted by a solenoidal field is not unique. If $\mathbf {A}$ is a vector potential for $\mathbf {v}$, then so is $$\mathbf {A} +\nabla f,$$where $f$ is any continuously differentiable scalar function. This follows from the fact that the curl of the gradient is zero. <p> This nonuniqueness leads to a degree of freedom in the formulation of electrodynamics, or gauge freedom, and requires [choosing a gauge](../../../../general/gauge%20fixing.md). <!--SR:!2025-09-07,136,423!2025-09-13,141,423-->
- [magnetic vector potential](../../../../general/magnetic%20vector%20potential.md) ::@:: It is the [vector quantity](vector%20quantity.md) defined so that its [curl](curl%20(mathematics).md) is equal to the [magnetic field](magnetic%20field.md): $\nabla \times \mathbf {A} =\mathbf {B}$. Together with the [electric potential](electric%20potential.md) _φ_, the magnetic vector potential can be used to specify the [electric field](electric%20field.md) __E__ as well. <!--SR:!2025-09-14,142,423!2025-09-24,147,423-->
  - magnetic vector potential / magnetostatics ::@:: In [magnetostatics](../../../../general/magnetostatics.md), if the Coulomb gauge $\ \nabla \cdot \mathbf {A} =0$ is imposed, then one can integrate to obtain the potentials: $$\mathbf {A} (\mathbf {r} )={\frac {\mu _{0} }{4\pi } }\int _{R}{\frac {\mathbf {J} (\mathbf {r} ')}{\left|\mathbf {r} -\mathbf {r} '\right|} }\mathrm {d} ^{3}r' \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-08-04,107,403!2025-07-01,78,383-->
    - magnetic vector potential / magnetostatics / derivation ::@:: Biot–Savart law can be rewritten into $$\mathbf B(\mathbf r) = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \nabla_{\mathbf r} \times \iiint_V \! \frac {\mathbf J(\mathbf \ell)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \,\mathrm dV \,.$$ We exclude the curl operator to define the vector potential. <!--SR:!2025-08-18,110,403!2026-04-16,295,383-->
    - magnetic vector potential / magnetostatics / analogy ::@:: In [magnetostatics](../../../../general/magnetostatics.md), if the Coulomb gauge $\ \nabla \cdot \mathbf {A} =0$ is imposed, then there is an analogy between $\mathbf {A} ,\mathbf {J}$ and $V,\rho$ in [electrostatics](../../../../general/electrostatics.md): $$\nabla ^{2}\mathbf {A} =-\mu _{0}\mathbf {J}$$ just like the electrostatic equation $$\nabla ^{2}V=-{\frac {\rho }{\epsilon _{0} } }$$ Likewise one can integrate to obtain the potentials: $$\mathbf {A} (\mathbf {r} )={\frac {\mu _{0} }{4\pi } }\int _{R}{\frac {\mathbf {J} (\mathbf {r} ')}{\left|\mathbf {r} -\mathbf {r} '\right|} }\mathrm {d} ^{3}r'$$ just like the equation for the [electric potential](../../../../general/electric%20potential.md): $$V(\mathbf {r} )={\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0} } }\int _{R}{\frac {\rho (\mathbf {r} ')}{|\mathbf {r} -\mathbf {r} '|} }\mathrm {d} ^{3}r'$$ <!--SR:!2026-01-05,194,363!2025-08-06,103,403-->
  - magnetic vector potential / gauge choices ::@:: The above definition does not define the magnetic vector potential uniquely because, by definition, we can arbitrarily add [curl](../../../../general/curl%20(mathematics).md)-free components to the magnetic potential without changing the observed magnetic field. Thus, there is a [degree of freedom](../../../../general/degrees%20of%20freedom%20(../../../../general/physics%20and%20chemistry).md) available when choosing $\mathbf {A}$. This condition is known as [gauge invariance](../../../../general/gauge%20invariance.md). <!--SR:!2025-09-18,146,423!2025-09-10,139,423-->
    - magnetic vector potential / gauge choices / common ::@:: The [Lorenz gauge](../../../../general/Lorenz%20gauge.md): $$\ \nabla \cdot \mathbf {A} +{\frac {1}{\ c^{2} } }{\frac {\partial \phi }{\partial t} }=0$$ <br/> The [Coulomb gauge](Coulomb%20gauge.md#Coulomb%20gauge): $$\ \nabla \cdot \mathbf {A} =0$$ <!--SR:!2025-08-19,111,403!2025-07-31,98,403-->

## week 7 tutorial

- datetime: 2025-03-20T18:00:00+08:00/2025-03-20T18:50:00+08:00
- topic: boundary value problem of magnetostatics
- magnetic moment
  - magnetic moment / models ::@:: Classically, magnetic moment can be modeled as a magnetic pole \(similar to electric dipoles\) or Ampèrian loop \(what we use nowadays\). <!--SR:!2025-09-25,148,423!2025-09-16,144,423-->
    - magnetic moments / models / magnetic pole ::@:: The sources of magnetic moments in materials can be represented by poles in analogy to [electrostatics](../../../../general/electrostatics.md). This is sometimes known as the Gilbert model. In this model, a small magnet is modeled by a pair of _fictitious_ magnetic monopoles of equal magnitude but opposite [polarity](../../../../general/magnetic%20polarity.md#polarity). Each pole is the source of magnetic force which weakens with distance. <p> The magnetic dipole moment __m__ is related to the fictitious poles as $$\mathbf {m} =p\,\mathrm {\boldsymbol {\ell } } \,.$$ It points in the direction from South to North pole. The analogy with electric dipoles should not be taken too far because magnetic dipoles are associated with [angular momentum](../../../../general/angular%20momentum.md). <!--SR:!2025-07-30,102,403!2025-07-30,102,403-->
    - magnetic moments / models / Ampèrian loop ::@:: In this model developed by Ampère, the elementary magnetic dipole that makes up all magnets is a sufficiently small amperian loop of current _I_. The dipole moment of this loop is $$\mathbf {m} =I{\boldsymbol {S} },$$ where _S_ is the area of the loop. The direction of the magnetic moment is in a direction normal to the area enclosed by the current consistent with the direction of the current using the right hand rule. <!--SR:!2025-08-17,109,403!2025-09-25,148,423-->
  - magnetic moment / motivation ::@:: Consider the dipole term of the multipole approximation, but for the magnetic vector potential: $$\mathbf A(\mathbf r) = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \sum_{k = 0}^\infty \frac 1 {r^{k + 1} } \iiint_V \! P_k(\cos \theta) \, \mathbf J(\mathbf \ell) \lvert \mathbf \ell \rvert^k \,\mathrm{d}V \,,$$ where $\theta$ is the angle between $\mathbf r$ and $\mathbf \ell$. <p> The monopole term should disappear because there are no magnetic monopoles. The dipole term, which is dominant unless zero, is: $$\mathbf A_{\text{dip} }(\mathbf r) = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \frac 1 {r^2} \iiint_V \! \cos \theta \, \mathbf J(\mathbf \ell) \lvert \mathbf \ell \rvert \,\mathrm dV = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \frac 1 {r^2} \iiint_V \! (\hat{\mathbf r} \cdot \mathbf \ell) \mathbf J(\mathbf \ell) \,\mathrm dV \,.$$ Now if we restrict the current density to a current-carrying loop of current $I$, then we rewrite the integral into a closed loop integral: $$\mathbf A_{\text{dip} }(\mathbf r) = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \frac I {r^2} \oint_C \! (\hat{\mathbf r} \cdot \mathbf \ell) \, \mathrm d\mathbf \ell$$ Using the identity $\oint_C \! (\mathbf v \cdot \mathbf \ell) \,\mathrm d\mathbf \ell = -\iint_A \! \mathbf v \times \mathrm d\mathbf A$ \(note that $\mathrm d\mathbf \ell$ points in the integration direction, which is different from $\mathbf \ell$\), we have $$\mathbf A_{\text{dip} }(\mathbf r) = -\frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \frac I {r^2} \hat{\mathbf r} \times \iint_A \! \mathrm d\mathbf A = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \frac {\mathbf m \times \hat {\mathbf r} } {r^2} \,,$$ where $$\mathbf m = I \iint_A \! \mathrm d\mathbf A = I \mathbf A$$ is the _magnetic dipole moment_ we are after. The direction of the area vector follows the right hand rule. <!--SR:!2025-07-03,64,323!2025-07-05,82,383-->
    - magnetic moment / motivation / identity ::@:: $$\oint_C \! (\mathbf v \cdot \mathbf \ell) \,\mathrm d\mathbf \ell = -\iint_A \! \mathbf v \times \mathrm d\mathbf A$$ \(note that $\mathrm d\mathbf \ell$ points in the integration direction, which is different from $\mathbf \ell$\) <!--SR:!2025-07-26,79,392!2025-07-02,6,312-->
  - magnetic moment / magnetic vector potential ::@:: The magnetic vector potential due to an ideal magnetic dipole $\mathbf m$ is $$\mathbf A(\mathbf r) = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \frac {\mathbf m \times \hat {\mathbf r} } {r^2} \,.$$ Assuming $\mathbf m$ points into the positive $z$-direction. In spherical coordinates: $$\mathbf A(r, \theta, \varphi) = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \frac {m \sin \theta} {r^2} \hat{\boldsymbol \varphi} \,.$$ <p> Take the curl to find its magnetic field. In spherical coordinates, we need to be careful when taking the curl \(very cursed\). We have: $$\begin{aligned} \mathbf B = \nabla \times \mathbf A & = \frac 1 {r^2 \sin \theta} \begin{vmatrix} \hat{\mathbf r} & r \hat{\boldsymbol \theta} & r \sin \theta \hat{\boldsymbol \varphi} \\ \frac {\partial} {\partial r} & \frac {\partial} {\partial \theta} & \frac {\partial} {\partial \varphi} \\ 0 & r 0 & r \sin \theta \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \frac {m \sin \theta} {r^2} \end{vmatrix} \\ & = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \frac m {r^3} \left(2 \cos \theta \hat{\mathbf r} + \sin \theta \hat{\boldsymbol \theta} \right) \,. \end{aligned}$$ Notice this is formally similar to that given by an electric dipole. <!--SR:!2025-10-09,133,343!2026-01-20,208,363-->

## week 8 lecture

- datetime: 2025-03-24T09:00:00+08:00/2025-03-24T10:20:00+08:00
- topic: magnetic moment, magnetostatics in materials
- [magnetism](../../../../general/magnetism.md) ::@:: It is the class of physical attributes that occur through a magnetic field, which allows objects to attract or repel each other. Because both electric currents and magnetic moments of elementary particles give rise to a magnetic field, magnetism is one of two aspects of electromagnetism. <!--SR:!2025-11-04,159,432!2025-11-06,161,433-->
  - magnetism / types ::@:: 3 common ones: diamagnetism, ferromagnetism, paramagnetism; there are more unmentioned here <!--SR:!2025-10-21,145,432!2025-10-27,151,433-->
- [paramagnetism](../../../../general/paramagnetism.md) ::@:: In a paramagnetic material there are unpaired electrons; i.e., atomic or molecular orbitals with exactly one electron in them. While paired electrons are required by the Pauli exclusion principle to have their intrinsic ('spin') magnetic moments pointing in opposite directions, causing their magnetic fields to cancel out, an unpaired electron is free to align its magnetic moment in any direction. When an external magnetic field is applied, these magnetic moments will tend to align themselves in the same direction as the applied field, thus reinforcing it. <!--SR:!2025-07-31,84,393!2025-08-29,114,412-->
  - paramagnetism / torque ::@:: Consider a rectangular current loop. In a magnetic field, the rectangular current loop experience a torque by $\boldsymbol \tau = \mathbf m \times \mathbf B$. <p> This can be derived by considering the force on two sides of the rectangular current loop. <!--SR:!2025-11-04,159,433!2025-10-29,153,433-->
- [diamagnetism](../../../../general/diamagnetism.md) ::@:: Diamagnetism appears in all materials and is the tendency of a material to oppose an applied magnetic field, and therefore, to be repelled by a magnetic field. <p> In a diamagnetic material, there are no unpaired electrons, so the intrinsic electron magnetic moments cannot produce any bulk effect \(no paramagnetism\). In these cases, the magnetization arises from the electrons' orbital motions, which can be understood classically. <!--SR:!2025-07-29,82,393!2025-11-04,159,433-->
  - diamagnetism / classical understanding ::@:: When a material is put in a magnetic field, the electrons circling the nucleus will experience, in addition to their Coulomb attraction to the nucleus, a Lorentz force from the magnetic field. Depending on which direction the electron is orbiting, this force may increase the centripetal force on the electrons, pulling them in towards the nucleus, or it may decrease the force, pulling them away from the nucleus. This effect systematically increases the orbital magnetic moments that were aligned opposite the field and decreases the ones aligned parallel to the field (in accordance with Lenz's law). This results in a small bulk magnetic moment, with an opposite direction to the applied field. <!--SR:!2025-07-26,79,392!2025-08-31,116,413-->
  - diamagnetism / paramagnetism ::@:: In a material with paramagnetic properties (that is, with a tendency to enhance an external magnetic field), the paramagnetic behavior dominates. Thus, despite its universal occurrence, diamagnetic behavior is observed only in a purely diamagnetic material. <!--SR:!2025-09-01,116,413!2025-10-26,150,432-->
- [ferromagnetism](../../../../general/ferromagnetism.md) ::@:: A ferromagnet, like a paramagnetic substance, has unpaired electrons. However, in addition to the electrons' intrinsic magnetic moment's tendency to be parallel to an applied field, there is also in these materials a tendency for these magnetic moments to orient parallel to each other to maintain a lowered-energy state. Thus, even in the absence of an applied field, the magnetic moments of the electrons in the material spontaneously line up parallel to one another. <!--SR:!2025-10-28,152,433!2025-10-19,143,432-->
  - ferromagnetism / examples ::@:: Ferromagnetism only occurs in a few substances; common ones are iron, nickel, cobalt, their alloys, and some alloys of rare-earth metals. <!--SR:!2025-10-24,148,433!2025-10-22,146,432-->
  - ferromagnetism / magnetic domains ::@:: Within each domain, the spins are aligned, but if the bulk material is in its lowest energy configuration (i.e. "unmagnetized"), the spins of separate domains point in different directions and their magnetic fields cancel out, so the bulk material has no net large-scale magnetic field. <!--SR:!2025-11-04,159,433!2025-11-05,160,432-->
  - ferromagnetism / magnetized materials ::@:: The magnetic domains in a material are not fixed in place; they are simply regions where the spins of the electrons have aligned spontaneously due to their magnetic fields, and thus can be altered by an external magnetic field. If a strong-enough external magnetic field is applied to the material, the domain walls will move via a process in which the spins of the electrons in atoms near the wall in one domain turn under the influence of the external field to face in the same direction as the electrons in the other domain, thus reorienting the domains so more of the dipoles are aligned with the external field. The domains will remain aligned when the external field is removed, and sum to create a magnetic field of their own extending into the space around the material, thus creating a "permanent" magnet. <!--SR:!2025-08-30,115,412!2025-10-21,145,432-->
    - ferromagnetism / magnetized materials / stability ::@:: The domains do not go back to their original minimum energy configuration when the field is removed because the domain walls tend to become 'pinned' or 'snagged' on defects in the crystal lattice, preserving their parallel orientation. <!--SR:!2025-10-27,151,433!2025-09-01,116,413-->
- [magnetization](../../../../general/magnetization.md) ::@:: It is the vector field that expresses the density of permanent or induced magnetic dipole moments in a magnetic material. Accordingly, physicists and engineers usually define magnetization as the quantity of magnetic moment per unit volume. It is represented by a pseudovector __M__. Magnetization can be compared to electric polarization, which is the measure of the corresponding response of a material to an electric field in electrostatics. <!--SR:!2025-07-30,83,393!2025-11-05,160,433-->
  - magnetization / definition ::@:: The magnetization field or __M__-field can be defined according to the following equation: $$\mathbf {M} ={\frac {\mathrm {d} \mathbf {m} }{\mathrm {d} V} }$$ Where $\mathrm {d} \mathbf {m}$ is the elementary [magnetic moment](../../../../general/magnetic%20moment.md) and  $\mathrm {d} V$ is the [volume element](../../../../general/volume%20element.md); in other words, the __M__-field is the distribution of magnetic moments in the region or [manifold](../../../../general/manifold.md) concerned. This is better illustrated through the following relation: $$\mathbf {m} =\iiint \mathbf {M} \,\mathrm {d} V$$ where __m__ is an ordinary magnetic moment and the triple integral denotes integration over a volume. <!--SR:!2025-11-05,160,432!2025-10-27,151,432-->
    - magnetization / definition / analogy ::@:: This makes the __M__-field completely analogous to the [electric polarization field](../../../../general/polarization%20density.md), or __P__-field, used to determine the [electric dipole moment](../../../../general/electric%20dipole%20moment.md) __p__ generated by a similar region or manifold with such a polarization: $$\mathbf {P} ={\mathrm {d} \mathbf {p} \over \mathrm {d} V},\quad \mathbf {p} =\iiint \mathbf {P} \,\mathrm {d} V,$$where $\mathrm {d} \mathbf {p}$ is the elementary electric dipole moment. <!--SR:!2025-10-23,147,432!2025-10-25,149,432-->
  - magnetization / unit ::@:: The __M__-field is measured in _[amperes](../../../../general/ampere.md) per [meter](../../../../general/meter.md)_ \(A/m\) in [SI units](../../../../general/SI%20units.md). <!--SR:!2025-09-01,116,413!2025-08-13,59,393-->
  - magnetization / magnetization current ::@:: The magnetic vector potential due to a magnetized material is: $$\mathbf A(\mathbf r) = \frac {\mu_0} {4 \pi} \iiint_V \! \frac {\mathbf M(\mathbf \ell) \times \mathbf (\mathbf r - \mathbf \ell) } {\lvert \mathbf r -\mathbf \ell \rvert^3} \,\mathrm dV \,.$$ Using the product rule for $\nabla \times$, we have $$\begin{aligned} \mathbf A(\mathbf r) & = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \iiint_V \! \mathbf M(\mathbf \ell) \times \nabla_{\mathbf \ell} \left(\frac 1 {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \right) \,\mathrm dV \\ & = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \iiint_V \! \frac 1 {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \nabla_{\mathbf \ell} \times \mathbf M(\mathbf \ell) \,\mathrm dV - \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \iiint_V \! \nabla_{\mathbf \ell} \times \left( \frac {\mathbf M(\mathbf \ell)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \right) \,\mathrm dV \,. \end{aligned}$$ Using the identity $\iiint_V \! \nabla \times \mathbf v \,\mathrm dV = -\oiint_S \! \mathbf v \times \mathrm d\mathbf A$, we have $$\mathbf A(\mathbf r) = \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \iiint_V \! \frac 1 {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \nabla_{\mathbf \ell} \times \mathbf M(\mathbf \ell) \,\mathrm dV + \frac {\mu_0} {4\pi} \oiint_S \! \frac 1 {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf \ell \rvert} \mathbf M(\mathbf \ell) \times \mathrm d\mathbf A \,.$$ We can identify $\nabla_{\mathbf \ell} \times \mathbf M(\mathbf \ell)$ with a bound volume current $\mathbf J_m$ and $\mathbf M(\mathbf \ell) \times \mathrm d\hat{\mathbf A}$ with a bound surface current $\mathbf K_m$. <!--SR:!2025-07-18,79,393!2025-07-01,62,373-->
    - magnetization / magnetization current / identity ::@:: $$\iiint_V \! \nabla \times \mathbf v \,\mathrm dV = -\oiint_S \! \mathbf v \times \mathrm d\mathbf S$$ This identity can be obtained from the generalized Stokes' theorem, where the integral of a differential form $\omega$ over the [boundary](../../../../general/boundary%20of%20a%20manifold.md#manifold%20with%20boundary) $\partial \Omega$ of some [orientable](../../../../general/orientation%20(vector%20space).md#orientation%20on%20manifolds) manifold $\Omega$ is equal to the integral of its [exterior derivative](../../../../general/exterior%20derivative.md) $d\omega$ over the whole of $\Omega$, i.e., $$\oint _{\partial \Omega } \! \lvert \mathrm dS \rvert \hat {\mathbf n} A =\int _{\Omega } \lvert \mathrm dV \rvert \nabla A \,.$$ When $A$ is a vector field $\mathbf A$, the equation splits into two parts, with one of them being the exterior product \(the other is similar but with the dot product\): $$\oint_{\partial \Omega} \! \lvert \mathrm dS \rvert \hat {\mathbf n} \wedge \mathbf A = \oint_{\Omega} \lvert \mathrm dV \rvert \nabla \wedge \mathbf A \,.$$ In three dimensions, using Hodge duality, we have $$\oint_{\partial \Omega} \! \lvert \mathrm dS \rvert \hat {\mathbf n} \times \mathbf A = \oint_\Omega \lvert \mathrm dV \rvert \nabla \times \mathbf A \,,$$ which is the above identity. <!--SR:!2025-06-27,58,372!2025-07-28,81,393-->
    - magnetization / magnetization current / intuition ::@:: In the Amperian loop model, the magnetization is due to combining many tiny Amperian loops to form a resultant current called _bound current_. This bound current, then, is the source of the magnetic __B__ field due to the magnet. <p> The above says a magnetized object can be replaced by a volume current $\mathbf J_m = \nabla \times \mathbf M$ \(_magnetization current density_\) through the material plus a surface current $\mathbf K_m = \mathbf M \times \hat {\mathbf n}$ on the material surface \(_polarization current density_\). We can think of a magnetized material consist of many Amperian loops. When the microscopic currents induced by the magnetization do not balance out, bound volume currents and bound surface currents appear in the medium. <!--SR:!2025-07-27,80,393!2025-07-30,83,393-->
    - magnetization / magnetization current / figure ::@:: ![magnetization current](../../../../archives/Wikimedia%20Commons/Bound%20currents.gif) <!--SR:!2025-10-27,151,433!2025-10-29,153,432-->
    - magnetization / magnetization current / Ampere's circuital law ::@:: For simplicity, we ignore the polarization current density \(as we will see later, the term due to the polarization current density can be considered part of Maxwell's correction for the magnetizing field\). Thus $\mathbf J_b = \mathbf J_m = \nabla \times \mathbf M$. Then we have $$\frac 1 {\mu_0} \nabla \times \mathbf B = \mathbf J_f + \mathbf J_b = \mathbf J_f + \nabla \times \mathbf M \implies \nabla \times \left(\frac 1 {\mu_0} \mathbf B - \mathbf M\right) = \mathbf J_f \implies \nabla \times \mathbf H = \mathbf J_f \,,$$ where $\mathbf H$ is the magnetizing field. \(Maxwell's correction is ignored here.\) <!--SR:!2025-10-23,147,432!2025-07-19,80,392-->
  - magnetization / magnetostatics ::@:: In the absence of free electric currents and time-dependent effects, [Maxwell's equations](../../../../general/Maxwell's%20equations.md) describing the magnetic quantities reduce to $${\begin{aligned}\mathbf {\nabla \times H} &=\mathbf {0} \\\mathbf {\nabla \cdot H} &=-\nabla \cdot \mathbf {M} \end{aligned} }$$ These equations can be solved in analogy with [electrostatic](../../../../general/electrostatic.md) problems where $${\begin{aligned}\mathbf {\nabla \times E} &=\mathbf {0} \\\mathbf {\nabla \cdot E} &={\frac {\rho }{\epsilon _{0} } }\end{aligned} }$$ In this sense −∇⋅<!-- markdown separator -->__M__ plays the role of a fictitious "magnetic charge density" analogous to the [electric charge density](../../../../general/electric%20charge%20density.md) _ρ_; \(see also [demagnetizing field](../../../../general/demagnetizing%20field.md)\). <!--SR:!2025-07-20,81,392!2025-07-26,79,392-->
- magnetic field
  - magnetic field / H-field ::@:: The magnetic __H__ field is defined: $$\mathbf {H} \equiv {\frac {1}{\mu _{0} } }\mathbf {B} -\mathbf {M}$$ where $\mu _{0}$ is the [vacuum permeability](../../../../general/vacuum%20permeability.md), and __M__ is the [magnetization vector](../../../../general/magnetization.md). <!--SR:!2025-10-22,146,432!2025-10-20,144,433-->
    - magnetic field / H-field / sign ::@:: Compare with $\mathbf D = \varepsilon_0 \mathbf E + \mathbf P$. One would find the sign of the second term reversed. This is because $\mathbf E$ points from positive to negative but $\mathbf P$ points from negative to positive, while both $\mathbf B$ and $\mathbf M$ point from south to north \(as dipole\). \(This is very cursed...\) <!--SR:!2025-10-19,143,433!2025-11-02,157,432-->
- [magnetic susceptibility](../../../../general/magnetic%20susceptibility.md) ::@:: It is a measure of how much a material will become magnetized in an applied magnetic field. It is the ratio of magnetization __M__ (magnetic moment per unit volume) to the applied magnetic field intensity __H__. <!--SR:!2025-08-28,112,412!2025-07-29,82,393-->
  - magnetic susceptibility / sign ::@:: This allows a simple classification, into two categories, of most materials' responses to an applied magnetic field: an alignment with the magnetic field, _χ_ > 0, called _paramagnetism_, or an alignment against the field, _χ_ < 0, called _diamagnetism_. <!--SR:!2025-11-04,159,433!2025-08-26,110,412-->
  - magnetic susceptibility / values ::@:: Typical values are around −10<sup>–5</sup>. Superconductors are perfect diamagnetics with _χ_<sub>_m_</sub> = −1. <!--SR:!2025-11-04,159,433!2025-11-04,159,432-->
  - magnetic susceptibility / relative permeability ::@:: The below assumes linear material. <p> Using [SI units](../../../../general/SI%20electromagnetism%20units.md), the [magnetic induction](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) __B__ is related to __H__ by the relationship $$\mathbf {B} =\mu _{0}(\mathbf {H} +\mathbf {M} )\ {\stackrel {\text{linear} }{=} }\ \mu _{0}(1+\chi _{\text{v} })\mathbf {H} =\mu \mathbf {H} ,$$ where _μ_<sub>0</sub> is the [vacuum permeability](../../../../general/vacuum%20permeability.md) \(see table of [physical constants](../../../../general/physical%20constant.md)\), and \(1 + _χ_<sub>v</sub>\) is the [relative permeability](../../../../general/permeability%20(electromagnetism).md#relative%20permeability%20and%20magnetic%20susceptibility) of the material. Thus the _volume magnetic susceptibility_ _χ_<sub>v</sub> and the [magnetic permeability](../../../../general/magnetic%20permeability.md) _μ_ are related by the following formula: $$\mu \ {\stackrel {\text{def} }{=} }\ \mu _{0}(1+\chi _{\text{v} }).$$ <!--SR:!2025-07-28,81,393!2025-07-26,79,392-->

## week 8 lecture 2

- datetime: 2025-03-26T09:00:00+08:00/2025-03-26T10:20:00+08:00
- topic: electrodynamics, Faraday's law, Lenz's law
- [Faraday's law of induction](../../../../general/Faraday's%20law%20of%20induction.md) ::@:: It is a law of electromagnetism predicting how a magnetic field will interact with an electric circuit to produce an electromotive force \(emf\). <!--SR:!2025-07-27,80,393!2025-10-22,146,433-->
  - Faraday's law of induction / motivation ::@:: Consider a rectangular loop, with one side in a uniform magnetic field. When we pull the rectangular loop out from the magnetic field, charges in the loop should experience Lorentz force. The emf generated is: $$\mathcal E = \frac W q = \frac {qvB \cdot l} q = vBl \,,$$ where $l$ is the length of that side of the rectangular loop. <p> Conveniently, this is also the decrease of the magnetic flux through the rectangular loop. So we have: $$\mathcal E = -\frac {\mathrm d \Phi_B} {\mathrm dt} \,.$$ In fact, this equation applies very generally to any loops, even changing loops \(assuming the velocity of the abstract curve ∂Σ matches the actual velocity of the material conducting the electricity\). <p> \(We also note that Lorentz force should not do any work, so where does the work come from? It turns out moving the loop provides the work.\) <!--SR:!2025-07-27,80,393!2025-07-01,62,372-->
  - Faraday's law of induction / mathematical statement ::@:: Faraday's law states that the emf is also given by the [rate of change](../../../../general/time%20derivative.md) of the magnetic flux: $${\mathcal {E} }=-{\frac {\mathrm {d} \Phi _{B} }{\mathrm {d} t} },$$ where ${\mathcal {E} }$ is the [electromotive force](../../../../general/electromotive%20force.md) \(emf\) and Φ<sub>_B_</sub> is the [magnetic flux](../../../../general/magnetic%20flux.md). <p> The direction of the electromotive force is given by [Lenz's law](../../../../general/Lenz's%20law.md). <!--SR:!2025-10-28,152,432!2025-11-02,157,432-->
    - Faraday's law of induction / mathematical statement / coil ::@:: For a tightly wound [coil of wire](../../../../general/inductor.md), composed of _N_ identical turns, each with the same Φ<sub>_B_</sub>, Faraday's law of induction states that $${\mathcal {E} }=-N{\frac {\mathrm {d} \Phi _{B} }{\mathrm {d} t} }$$ where _N_ is the number of turns of wire and Φ<sub>_B_</sub> is the magnetic flux through a single loop. <!--SR:!2025-11-03,158,433!2025-11-05,160,432-->
- [electromagnetic induction](../../../../general/electromagnetic%20induction.md) ::@:: It is the production of an electromotive force (emf) across an electrical conductor in a changing magnetic field. <!--SR:!2025-10-24,148,433!2025-11-06,161,433-->
  - electromagnetic induction / experiment ::@:: You have a coil of wire connected to an ammeter. When you move an magnet/electromagnet nearby, it generates current. Moving faster increases the current. Moving in the opposite direction reverses the current. Changing the electromagnet strength generates current as well. <p> To summarize, change in magnetic field through the coil of wire induces electric current. <!--SR:!2025-11-03,158,433!2025-11-06,161,433-->
- Faraday's law of induction
  - Faraday's law of induction / Maxwell–Faraday equation ::@:: It is $$\nabla \times \mathbf {E} =-{\frac {\partial \mathbf {B} }{\partial t} }$$ \(in [SI units](../../../../general/SI%20units.md)\) where ∇ × is the [curl](../../../../general/curl%20(mathematics).md) [operator](../../../../general/linear%20operator.md) and again __E__\(__r__, _t_\) is the [electric field](../../../../general/electric%20field.md) and __B__\(__r__, _t_\) is the [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md). These fields can generally be functions of position __r__ and time _t_. <p> It can be considered as the differential form of Faraday's law, by Stokes' theorem. This is also one of the four Maxwell's equations. <!--SR:!2025-10-25,149,433!2025-09-01,116,413-->
- [Lenz's law](../../../../general/Lenz's%20law.md) ::@:: It states that the direction of the electric current induced in a conductor by a changing magnetic field is such that the magnetic field created by the induced current opposes changes in the initial magnetic field. <!--SR:!2025-08-27,111,412!2025-10-28,152,432-->
  - Lenz's law / intuition ::@:: We can think of the electrical loop liking to maintain a constant flux through itself. It does not succeed completely, as the flux produced is typically a tiny fraction of the change in magnetic field. <!--SR:!2025-11-06,161,433!2025-10-20,144,433-->
- Faraday's law of induction
  - Faraday's law of induction / pure Faraday field ::@:: We now know that $\nabla \times \mathbf E = -\frac {\partial \mathbf B} {\partial t}$. A pure Faraday field is an electric field that is generated purely by changes in magnetic field. That is, it satisfies the above, and it has no electric charge density, thus $\nabla \cdot \mathbf E = 0$. <p> In this case, we observe such an electric field is completely analogous to the magnetic field, but with $-\frac {\partial \mathbf B} {\partial t}$ replacing $\mu_0 \mathbf J$. <p> We can obtain $$\oint \mathbf E \cdot \,\mathrm d\mathbf \ell = -\frac {\mathrm d\Phi_B} {\mathrm dt} \,,$$ similar to $$\oint \mathbf B \cdot \,\mathrm d\mathbf \ell = \mu_0 I_{\text{enc} } \,.$$ We see this induced electric field is responsible for inducing the electric current in the loop. Indeed, it performs work of $\mathcal E = -\frac {\mathrm d\Phi_B} {\mathrm dt}$ per unit charge when moving charges once around the loop. <!--SR:!2025-07-02,63,372!2025-06-30,61,373-->

- note
  - note / gradient field ::@:: \(__this course__: Divergence is nonzero. Curl is zero.\) <!--SR:!2025-08-31,116,413!2025-10-23,147,433-->
  - note / rotating field ::@:: \(__this course__: Divergence is zero. Curl is nonzero.\) <!--SR:!2025-10-28,152,433!2025-10-26,150,432-->
- [metric prefix](../../../../general/metric%20prefix.md) ::@:: It is a unit prefix that precedes a basic unit of measure to indicate a multiple or submultiple of the unit. All metric prefixes used today are decadic. Each prefix has a unique symbol that is prepended to any unit symbol. <!--SR:!2025-08-31,115,412!2025-10-26,150,433-->
  - metric prefix / common prefixes ::@:: \(in increasing order of magnitude, each separated by 3 OoMs\) femto-/f ← pico-/p ← nano-/n ← micro-/μ ← milli-/m ← \(unit\) → kilo/k → mega/M → giga/G → tera/T → peta/P <!--SR:!2025-08-25,109,412!2025-07-26,79,392-->

  - Taylor's theorem \(–1\) ::@:: $\alpha$ and $\mathbf r'$ are constanst. Approximate the Coulomb potential $V(\mathbf r) = \frac {\alpha} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert}$ in two dimensions around the origin up to the third order of $\mathbf r'$ \(second order terms\). <p> Be a bit more careful with multipole expansion. Or learn to use Taylor's theorem for multivariate functions, as the solution uses gradient and the Hessian matrix to derive the expression. <p> \(After looking at the derivation for three dimensions below, it seems _really_ terrible in examinations to use Taylor's theorem instead of multipole expansion...\) <!--SR:!2025-11-08,163,441!2025-10-24,148,441-->
  - radial dependence \(–1\) ::@:: When the question says to state the radial dependence on a graph, write down the proportionality with regards to the radius on the graph. <!--SR:!2025-11-04,159,440!2025-11-05,160,440-->
  - proportionality \(–6\) ::@:: Always think of proportionality as $$y = kx$$ when manipulating to avoid mistakes such as subtracting proportionality and solving for unknowns directly. Quite costly. Let's try better next time... <p> And try to be more sensitive to abornomally large or small answers compared to values in the question \(e.g. 265&nbsp;cm vs. 0.4&nbsp;cm and 0.8&nbsp;cm\). At least check said values. <!--SR:!2025-11-07,162,440!2025-11-03,158,441-->
