## week 10 lecture

- datetime: 2025-04-07T09:00:00+08:00/2025-04-07T10:20:00+08:00, PT1H20M
- topic: displacement current, Maxwell's equations
- [Maxwell's equations](../../../../general/Maxwell's%20equations.md) ::@:: They are are a set of coupled partial differential equations that, together with the Lorentz force law, form the foundation of classical electromagnetism, classical optics, electric and magnetic circuits. <!--SR:!2025-11-06,161,441!2025-11-09,164,441-->
  - Maxwell's equations / microscopic ::@:: \(top to bottom: Gauss's law, Gauss's law for magnetism, Faraday's law, Ampère-Maxwell law\) $${\begin{aligned}\nabla \cdot \mathbf {E} \,\,\,&={\frac {\rho }{\varepsilon _{0} } }\\\nabla \cdot \mathbf {B} \,\,\,&=0\\\nabla \times \mathbf {E} &=-{\frac {\partial \mathbf {B} }{\partial t} }\\\nabla \times \mathbf {B} &=\mu _{0}\left(\mathbf {J} +\varepsilon _{0}{\frac {\partial \mathbf {E} }{\partial t} }\right)\end{aligned} }$$ With $\mathbf {E}$ the electric field, $\mathbf {B}$ the magnetic field, $\rho$ the [electric charge density](../../../../general/electric%20charge%20density.md) and $\mathbf {J}$ the [current density](../../../../general/current%20density.md). $\varepsilon _{0}$ is the [vacuum permittivity](../../../../general/vacuum%20permittivity.md) and $\mu _{0}$ the [vacuum permeability](../../../../general/vacuum%20permeability.md). <!--SR:!2025-09-27,121,421!2025-10-31,155,440-->
  - Maxwell's equations / macroscopic ::@:: \(top to bottom: Gauss's law, Gauss's law for magnetism, Faraday's law, Ampère-Maxwell law\) $$\begin{aligned} \nabla \cdot \mathbf D & = \rho_f \\ \nabla \cdot \mathbf B & = 0 \\ \nabla \times \mathbf E & = -\frac {\partial \mathbf B} {\partial t} \\ \nabla \times \mathbf H & = \mathbf J_f + \frac {\partial \mathbf D} {\partial t} \end{aligned}$$ With $\mathbf {E}$ the electric field, $\mathbf D$ the displacement field, $\mathbf {B}$ the magnetic field, $\mathbf H$ the magnetizing field, $\rho_f$ the _free_ [electric charge density](../../../../general/electric%20charge%20density.md) and $\mathbf {J}_f$ the _free_ [current density](../../../../general/current%20density.md). $\varepsilon _{0}$ is the [vacuum permittivity](../../../../general/vacuum%20permittivity.md) and $\mu _{0}$ the [vacuum permeability](../../../../general/vacuum%20permeability.md). <!--SR:!2025-11-07,162,440!2025-10-31,155,440-->
  - Maxwell's equations / symmetry ::@:: If we assume magnetic monopoles exist, then we have a more symmetric set of equations \(Weber convention\): $$\begin{aligned} \nabla \cdot \mathbf E & = \frac {\rho_e} {\varepsilon_0} \\ \nabla \cdot \mathbf B & = \mu_0 \rho_m \\ \nabla \times \mathbf E & = -\mu_0 \mathbf j_m - \frac {\partial \mathbf B} {\partial t} \\ \nabla \times \mathbf B & = \mu_0 \mathbf j_e + \mu_0 \varepsilon_0 \frac {\partial \mathbf E} {\partial t} = \mu_0 \mathbf j_e + \frac 1 {c^2} \frac {\partial \mathbf E} {\partial t} \,, \end{aligned}$$ with the Lorentz force equation becoming: $$\mathbf F = q_e (\mathbf E + \mathbf v \times \mathbf B) + q_m \left(\mathbf B - \mathbf v \times \frac {\mathbf E} {c^2} \right)\,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-08-14,60,388!2025-08-30,65,390-->
- Ampère's circuital law
  - Ampère's circuital law / Maxwell's correction ::@:: The original equation is simply: $$\nabla \times \mathbf B = \mu_0 \mathbf J \,.$$ Taking its divergence, we expect it to be zero: $$\nabla \cdot (\nabla \times \mathbf B) = \mu_0 (\nabla \cdot \mathbf J) \overset ? = 0 \,.$$ But the divergence of current density is not necessarily zero. So we correct for it by subtracting the divergence of current density: $$\mu_0 (\nabla \cdot \mathbf J) = -\mu_0 \frac {\partial \rho} {\partial t} = -\mu_0 \varepsilon_0\frac {\partial} {\partial t} \nabla \cdot \mathbf E = \nabla \cdot \left(-\mu_0 \varepsilon_0 \frac {\partial \mathbf E} {\partial t} \right) \,.$$ Finally, we have: $$\nabla \times \mathbf B = \mu_0 \left(\mathbf J + \varepsilon_0 \frac {\partial \mathbf E} {\partial t} \right) \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-08-18,81,400!2025-09-19,113,421-->
    - Ampère's circuital law / Maxwell's correction / intuition ::@:: Consider a capacitor releasing current along a wire. Consider a loop surrouding the wire. Applying the uncorrected law to the circle enclosed by the loop yields a nonzero value due to the current. Applying the uncorrected law to a balloon surface enclosed by the same loop and evades the electric source and the wire, i.e. in between the capacitor plates, yields a zero value. <!--SR:!2025-11-09,164,441!2025-11-05,160,440-->
- [displacement current](../../../../general/displacement%20current.md) ::@:: It is the quantity ∂<!-- markdown separator -->__D__/∂<!-- markdown separator -->_t_ appearing in [Maxwell's equations](../../../../general/Maxwell's%20equations.md) that is defined in terms of the rate of change of __D__, the [electric displacement field](../../../../general/electric%20displacement%20field.md). <!--SR:!2025-11-06,161,441!2025-10-29,153,440-->
  - displacement current / interpretation ::@:: Displacement current density has the same units as electric current density, and it is a source of the [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) just as actual current is. However it is not an electric current of moving [charges](../../../../general/electric%20charge.md), but a time-varying [electric field](../../../../general/electric%20field.md). In physical materials \(as opposed to vacuum\), there is also a contribution from the slight motion of charges bound in atoms, called [dielectric polarization](../../../../general/dielectric%20polarization.md#dielectric%20polarisation). <!--SR:!2025-11-07,162,441!2025-11-07,162,440-->
  - displacement current / equation ::@:: Differentiating the electric displacement field with respect to time defines the _displacement current density_, which therefore has two components in a [dielectric](../../../../general/dielectric.md): $$\mathbf {J} _{\mathrm {D} }=\varepsilon _{0}{\frac {\partial \mathbf {E} }{\partial t} }+{\frac {\partial \mathbf {P} }{\partial t} }\,.$$ The first term on the right hand side is present in material media and in free space. It doesn't necessarily come from any actual movement of charge, but it does have an associated magnetic field, just as a current does due to charge motion. Some authors apply the name _displacement current_ to the first term by itself. \(__this course__: The name _displacement current_ is used this way during introduction.\) <!--SR:!2025-11-02,157,440!2025-10-30,154,440-->
  - displacement current / polarization current density ::@:: The second term on the right hand side, called polarization current density, comes from the change in [polarization](../../../../general/electric%20polarization.md) of the individual molecules of the dielectric material. A changing state of polarization corresponds to charge movement and so is equivalent to a current, hence the term "polarization current". Thus, $$I_{\mathrm {D} }=\iint _{S}\mathbf {J} _{\mathrm {D} }\cdot \operatorname {d} \!\mathbf {S} =\iint _{S}{\frac {\partial \mathbf {D} }{\partial t} }\cdot \operatorname {d} \!\mathbf {S} ={\frac {\partial }{\partial t} }\iint _{S}\mathbf {D} \cdot \operatorname {d} \!\mathbf {S} ={\frac {\partial \Phi _{\mathrm {D} } }{\partial t} }\,.$$ This polarization is the displacement current as it was originally conceived by Maxwell. <!--SR:!2025-09-24,118,421!2025-07-05,58,380-->
    - displacement current / polarization current density / magnetizing field ::@:: Remember when we derive the magnetizing field, we considered the curl of the magnetization field $\nabla \times \mathbf M$ and ignored the bound surface current: $\mathbf M \times \hat{\mathbf n}$, where $\hat{\mathbf n}$ is the surface outward normal. It turns out this bound surface current can be identified with the displacement current: $\frac {\partial \mathbf P} {\partial t}$. <p> Now we derive, considering it: $$\begin{aligned} \nabla \times \left(\frac 1 {\mu_0} \mathbf B\right) & = \mathbf J + \varepsilon_0 \frac {\partial \mathbf E} {\partial t} \\ & = \mathbf J_f + \nabla \times \mathbf M + \mathbf M \times \hat{\mathbf n} + \varepsilon_0 \frac {\partial \mathbf E} {\partial t} \\ & = \mathbf J_f + \nabla \times \mathbf M + \frac {\partial \mathbf P} {\partial t} + \varepsilon_0 \frac {\partial \mathbf E} {\partial t} \\ & = \mathbf J_f + \nabla \times \mathbf M + \frac {\partial \mathbf D} {\partial t} \\ \nabla \times \left(\frac 1 \mu_0 \mathbf B - \mathbf M \right) & = \mathbf J_f + \frac {\partial \mathbf D} {\partial t} \\ \nabla \times \mathbf H & = \mathbf J_f + \frac {\partial \mathbf D} {\partial t} \,. \end{aligned}$$ <!--SR:!2025-09-26,120,421!2025-09-28,122,421-->
  - displacement current / wave propagation ::@:: It leads to electricmagnetic wave propagation. Assuming no free and bound current, we have the displacement current: $$\mathbf J_D = \varepsilon_0 \frac {\partial \mathbf E} {\partial t} \,.$$ Some manipulation leads to a wave equation for the magnetic field: $$\frac {\partial^2 \mathbf B} {\partial t^2} = c^2 \nabla^2 \mathbf B \,,$$ and for the electric field: $$\frac {\partial^2 \mathbf E} {\partial t^2} = c^2 \nabla^2 \mathbf E \,,$$ where $c$ is the speed of light in vacuum. <!--SR:!2025-10-31,155,440!2025-11-08,163,440-->
- Maxwell's equations
  - Maxwell's equations / Lorentz force law ::@:: It, along with Maxwell's equations summarize, classical electrodynamics. <p> It states that a particle of charge _q_ moving with a velocity __v__ in an [electric field](../../../../general/electric%20field.md) __E__ and a [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) __B__ experiences a force \(in [SI units](../../../../general/International%20System%20of%20Units.md) of $$\mathbf {F} =q\left(\mathbf {E} +\mathbf {v} \times \mathbf {B} \right).$$ <!--SR:!2025-10-25,149,440!2025-10-30,154,440-->

## week 10 lecture 2

- datetime: 2025-04-09T09:00:00+08:00/2025-04-09T10:20:00+08:00, PT1H20M
- topic: magnetic inductance, energy of the magnetic field
- [solenoid](../../../../general/solenoid.md) ::@:: It is a type of electromagnet formed by a helical coil of wire whose length is substantially greater than its diameter, which generates a controlled magnetic field. The coil can produce a uniform magnetic field in a volume of space when an electric current is passed through it. <!--SR:!2025-11-05,160,441!2025-11-01,156,441-->
  - solenoid / infinite continuous solenoid ::@:: We get $$B=\mu _{0}{\frac {NI}{l} } \,.$$ This equation is valid for a solenoid in free space, which means the [permeability](../../../../general/permeability%20(electromagnetism).md) of the magnetic path is the same as permeability of free space, μ<sub>0</sub>. <p> The inclusion of a [ferromagnetic](../../../../general/ferromagnetic.md) core, such as [iron](../../../../general/iron.md), increases the magnitude of the magnetic flux density in the solenoid and raises the effective permeability of the magnetic path. This is expressed by the formula $$B=\mu _{0}\mu _{\mathrm {eff} }{\frac {NI}{l} }=\mu {\frac {NI}{l} },$$ where _μ_<sub>eff</sub> is the effective or apparent permeability of the core. <!--SR:!2025-11-03,158,441!2025-11-06,161,440-->
    - solenoid / infinite continuous solenoid / derivation ::@:: Applying [Ampère's circuital law](../../../../general/Ampère's%20circuital%20law.md) to the solenoid \(see figure below\) gives us $$Bl=\mu _{0}NI,$$ where $B$ is the [magnetic flux density](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md), $l$ is the length of the solenoid, $\mu _{0}$ is the [magnetic constant](../../../../general/magnetic%20constant.md), $N$ the number of turns, and $I$ the current. <p> ![How [Ampère's law](../../../../general/Ampère's%20circuital%20law.md) can be applied to the solenoid](../../../../archives/Wikimedia%20Commons/Solenoid%20and%20Ampere%20Law%20-%202.png) <!--SR:!2025-11-08,163,440!2025-09-19,113,420-->
    - solenoid / infinite continuous solenoid / inductance ::@:: Since $B = \mu_0 NI / l$, this implies a _flux linkage_ of: $\Psi = \mu_0 N^2 I A / l$. Using the definition of inductance, we have: $$L = \frac {\Psi} I = \mu_0 \frac {N^2 A} l \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-10-07,104,410!2025-07-02,28,410-->
- [flux linkage](../../../../general/flux%20linkage.md) ::@:: It is used to define the interaction of a multi-turn inductor with the magnetic flux as described by the Faraday's law of induction. <!--SR:!2025-11-08,163,440!2025-11-09,164,441-->
  - flux linkage / simplification ::@:: Since the contributions of all turns in the coil add up, in the over-simplified situation of the same flux $\Phi$ passing through all the turns, the flux linkage \(also known as _flux linked_\) is $\Psi =n\Phi$, where $n$ is the number of turns. <!--SR:!2025-10-30,154,440!2025-11-07,162,441-->
- [inductance](../../../../general/inductance.md) ::@:: It is the tendency of an electrical conductor to oppose a change in the electric current flowing through it. <!--SR:!2025-11-04,159,440!2025-11-08,163,441-->
  - inductance / mechanism ::@:: The electric current produces a [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) around the conductor. The magnetic field strength depends on the magnitude of the electric current, and therefore follows any changes in the magnitude of the current. From [Faraday's law of induction](../../../../general/Faraday's%20law%20of%20induction.md), any change in magnetic field through a circuit induces an [electromotive force](../../../../general/electromotive%20force.md) \(EMF\) \([voltage](../../../../general/voltage.md)\) in the conductors, a process known as [electromagnetic induction](../../../../general/electromagnetic%20induction.md). This induced voltage created by the changing current has the effect of opposing the change in current. This is stated by [Lenz's law](../../../../general/Lenz's%20law.md), and the voltage is called _[back EMF](../../../../general/back%20EMF.md)_. <!--SR:!2025-09-18,112,420!2025-11-01,156,441-->
  - inductance / analogy ::@:: Capacitance as to capacitors is analogous to inductance as to inductors. These two quantities both are determined solely by the geometry of the inductor. <!--SR:!2025-11-01,156,440!2025-11-07,162,440-->
  - inductance / SI unit ::@:: It is the [henry](../../../../general/henry%20(unit).md) \(H\), which is the amount of inductance that causes a voltage of one [volt](../../../../general/volt.md), when the current is changing at a rate of one [ampere](../../../../general/ampere%20(unit).md) per second. <!--SR:!2025-09-20,114,421!2025-08-20,83,401-->
  - inductance / two wire loops ::@:: The mutual inductance by a filamentary circuit $m$ on a filamentary circuit $n$ is given by the double integral _[Neumann](Franz%20Ernst%20Neumann.md) formula_ $$L_{m,n}={\frac {\mu _{0} }{4\pi } }\oint _{C_{m} }\oint _{C_{n} }{\frac {\mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} _{m}\cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} _{n} }{\ \left|\mathbf {x} _{m}-\mathbf {x} _{n}\right|\ } }\ ,$$ where <p> - $C_{m}$ and $C_{n}$ are the curves followed by the wires. <br/> - $\mu _{0}$ is the [permeability of free space](../../../../general/permeability%20of%20free%20space.md) \(4<!-- markdown separator -->_π_<!-- markdown separator -->×10<sup>−7</sup> H/m\) <br/> - $\mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} _{m}$ is a small increment of the wire in circuit _C_<sub>m</sub> <br/> - $\mathbf {x} _{m}$ is the position of $\mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} _{m}$ in space <br/> - $\mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} _{n}$ is a small increment of the wire in circuit _C_<sub>n</sub> <br/> - $\mathbf {x} _{n}$ is the position of $\mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} _{n}$ in space. <!--SR:!2025-11-05,160,440!2025-07-06,59,381-->
    - inductance / two wire loops / derivation ::@:: $$M_{ij}\mathrel {\stackrel {\mathrm {def} }{=} } {\frac {\Phi _{ij} }{I_{j} } }$$ where <p> - $I_{j}$ is the current through the $j$ th wire, this current creates the magnetic flux $\Phi _{ij}\ \,$ through the $i$ th surface <br/> - $\Phi _{ij}$ is the [magnetic flux](../../../../general/magnetic%20flux.md) through the _i_<!-- markdown separator -->th surface due to the [electrical circuit](../../../../general/electrical%20circuit.md) outlined by $C_{j}$: $$\Phi _{ij}=\int _{S_{i} }\mathbf {B} _{j}\cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {a} =\int _{S_{i} }(\nabla \times \mathbf {A_{j} } )\cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {a} =\oint _{C_{i} }\mathbf {A} _{j}\cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {s} _{i}=\oint _{C_{i} }\left({\frac {\mu _{0}I_{j} }{4\pi } }\oint _{C_{j} }{\frac {\mathrm {d} \mathbf {s} _{j} }{\left|\mathbf {s} _{i}-\mathbf {s} _{j}\right|} }\right)\cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {s} _{i}$$ where <p> - $C_{i}$ is the curve enclosing surface $S_{i}$; and $S_{i}$ is any arbitrary orientable area with edge $C_{i}$ <br/> - $\mathbf {B} _{j}$ is the [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) vector due to the $j$-th current \(of circuit $C_{j}$\). <br/> - $\mathbf {A} _{j}$ is the [vector potential](../../../../general/vector%20potential.md) due to the $j$-th current. <!--SR:!2025-07-11,64,381!2025-09-26,120,421-->
    - inductance / two wire loops / interpretation ::@:: We see the above expression is symmetric: It does not matter which loop has the current going through. We also see inductance only depends on the geometry of the two wires. We can consider these two wires as an _mutual inductor_ \(this ignores the self-inductance of the two wires\). <p> We also see why _mutual inductance_ is the emf induced in one coil \(secondary\) per unit time rate of change of current in the neighboring coil \(primary\): $$\mathcal E_2 = -M \frac {\partial i_1} {\partial t} \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-11-06,161,441!2025-09-27,121,420-->
  - inductance / mutual inductance ::@:: It is the emf induced in one coil \(secondary\) per unit time rate of change of current in the neighboring coil \(primary\): $$\mathcal E_2 = -M \frac {\partial i_1} {\partial t} \,.$$ <p> Integrating the above over time, and considering the initial condition where zero current implies zero magnetic flux, we have an equivalent definition: It is the flux linkage of one coil \(secondary\) per unit current in the neighboring coil \(primary\): $$\Phi_2 = -M i_1 \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-09-14,108,421!2025-09-21,115,420-->
    - inductance / mutual inductance / interpretation ::@:: The second definition gives an interpretation that an mutual inductor \(this ignores the self-inductance\) _stores_ magnetic flux proportional to the current running through it, and the proportionality constant is the mutual inductance \(this ignores the self-inductance\). <!--SR:!2025-11-05,160,440!2025-11-01,156,440-->
  - inductance / self inductance ::@:: The magnetic flux change also induces an emf in the source loop. Again, a quantity similar to mutual inductance can be defined: _self inductance_, or simply _inductance_. Its definition is also very similar: the _back emf_ induced per unit time rate of change of current: $$\mathcal E = -L \frac {\partial I} {\partial t} \,,$$ or equivalently, flux through the loop per unit current: $$\Phi_B = -LI \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-11-04,159,441!2025-11-03,158,441-->
    - inductance / self inductance / interpretation ::@:: The second definition gives an interpretation that an inductor _stores_ magnetic flux proportional to the current running through it, and the proportionality constant is the inductance. <p> Since the current fight against the emf, the used energy is stored in the stored magnetic flux, and is recoverable when you stop the current. So an inductor also _stores_ energy in its magnetic field. <!--SR:!2025-11-07,162,440!2025-10-30,154,440-->
    - inductance / self inductance / energy ::@:: The [energy](../../../../general/energy.md) $U$ \(measured in [joules](../../../../general/joule.md), in [SI](../../../../general/SI.md)\) stored by an inductance with a current $I$ through it is equal to the amount of work required to establish the current through the inductance from zero, and therefore the magnetic field. This is given by: $$U=\int _{0}^{I}L(i)\,i\,{\text{d} }i\,$$ If the inductance $L(i)$ is constant over the current range, the stored energy is $${\begin{aligned}U&=L\int _{0}^{I}\,i\,{\text{d} }i\\[3pt]&={\tfrac {1}{2} }L\,I^{2}\end{aligned} }$$ Inductance is therefore also proportional to the energy stored in the magnetic field for a given current. This energy is stored as long as the current remains constant. <!--SR:!2025-09-15,109,421!2025-11-08,163,440-->
- magnetic field
  - magnetic field / stored energy ::@:: Energy is needed to generate a magnetic field both to work against the electric field that a changing magnetic field creates and to change the magnetization of any material within the magnetic field. For non-dispersive materials, this same energy is released when the magnetic field is destroyed so that the energy can be modeled as being stored in the magnetic field. <!--SR:!2025-10-31,155,441!2025-11-08,163,441-->
- [magnetic energy](../../../../general/magnetic%20energy.md) ::@:: There are different ways to define the magnetic energy in different situations: magnetic dipole in a magnetic field, inductor, magnetic field, magnetostatic system of currents, etc. <!--SR:!2025-11-03,158,441!2025-11-06,161,440-->
  - magnetic energy / magnetic dipole in a magnetic field ::@:: It of a [magnet](../../../../general/magnet.md) or [magnetic moment](../../../../general/magnetic%20moment.md) $\mathbf {m}$ in a [magnetic field](../../../../general/magnetic%20field.md) $\mathbf {B}$ is defined as the [mechanical work](../../../../general/mechanical%20work.md) of the magnetic force on the re-alignment of the vector of the [magnetic dipole moment](../../../../general/magnetic%20dipole%20moment.md) and is equal to: $$E_{\text{p,m} }=-\mathbf {m} \cdot \mathbf {B}$$ The mechanical work takes the form of a torque ${\boldsymbol {N} }$: $$\mathbf {N} =\mathbf {m} \times \mathbf {B} =-\mathbf {r} \times \mathbf {\nabla } E_{\text{p,m} }$$ which will act to "realign" the magnetic dipole with the magnetic field. <!--SR:!2025-09-16,110,420!2025-07-09,41,380-->
  - magnetic energy / inductor ::@:: In an [electronic circuit](../../../../general/electronic%20circuit.md) the energy stored in an [inductor](../../../../general/inductor.md) \(of [inductance](../../../../general/inductance.md) $L$\) when a current $I$ flows through it is given by: $$E_{\text{p,m} }={\frac {1}{2} }LI^{2}.$$ This expression forms the basis for superconducting magnetic energy storage. It can be derived from a time average of the product of current and voltage across an inductor. <!--SR:!2025-11-09,164,441!2025-11-02,157,440-->
    - magnetic energy / inductor / analogy ::@:: Compare the energy of an inductor with the energy of a capacitor: $$\begin{aligned} E & = \frac 1 2 LI^2 \\ E & = \frac 1 2 CV^2 \,. \end{aligned}$$ <!--SR:!2025-12-05,162,448!2025-06-27,29,430-->
  - magnetic energy / magnetic field ::@:: Energy is also stored in a magnetic field itself. The energy per unit volume $u$ in a region of free space with [vacuum permeability](../../../../general/vacuum%20permeability.md) $\mu _{0}$ containing magnetic field $\mathbf {B}$ is: $$u={\frac {1}{2} }{\frac {B^{2} }{\mu _{0} } } \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-11-03,158,441!2025-11-09,164,441-->
    - magnetic energy / magnetic field / linear ::@:: More generally, if we assume that the medium is [paramagnetic](../../../../general/paramagnetism.md) or [diamagnetic](../../../../general/diamagnetism.md) so that a linear constitutive equation exists that relates $\mathbf {B}$ and the [magnetization](../../../../general/magnetization.md) $\mathbf {H}$ \(for example $\mathbf {H} =\mathbf {B} /\mu$ where $\mu$ is the [magnetic permeability](../../../../general/permeability%20(electromagnetism).md) of the material\), then it can be shown that the magnetic field stores an energy of $$E={\frac {1}{2} }\int \mathbf {H} \cdot \mathbf {B} \,\mathrm {d} V$$ where the integral is evaluated over the entire region where the magnetic field exists. <p> Alternative equations: $$u = \frac {\mathbf B \cdot \mathbf H} {2} = \frac {\mathbf B \cdot \mathbf B} {2\mu} = \frac {\mu \mathbf H \cdot \mathbf H} 2 \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-10-25,149,441!2025-09-22,116,421-->
    - magnetic energy / magnetic field / derivation ::@:: Start with the equation from a magnetostatic system of currents: $$\begin{aligned} u & = \frac 1 2 \int \! \mathbf J \cdot \mathbf A \,\mathrm dV \\ & = \frac 1 {2\mu_0} \int \! (\nabla \times \mathbf B) \cdot \mathbf A \,\mathrm dV \\ & = \frac 1 {2\mu_0} \int \! \left(\nabla \cdot (\mathbf B \times \mathbf A) + (\nabla \times \mathbf A) \cdot \mathbf B \right) \,\mathrm dV \\ & = \frac 1 {2\mu_0} \int \! \lvert \mathbf B \rvert^2 \,\mathrm dV + \frac 1 {2\mu_0} \oint \! (\mathbf B \times \mathbf A) \cdot \mathrm d\mathbf A \\ & = \frac 1 {2\mu_0} \int \! \lvert \mathbf B \rvert^2 \,\mathrm dV \,. \end{aligned}$$ The surface integral equals zero because as the surface approaches infinity, $\lvert \mathbf B \rvert \lvert \mathbf A \rvert \sim \frac 1 {r^3}$ while the surface area $A \sim r^2$. <!--SR:!2025-09-28,122,420!2025-10-29,153,440-->
    - magnetic energy / magnetic field / usage ::@:: Using symmetry, we can calculate the magnetic energy in the magnetic field of an _inductor_. Then we apply $u = \frac 1 2 L I^2$ to calculate its _inductance_. <!--SR:!2025-11-09,164,441!2025-11-03,158,441-->
  - magnetic energy / magnetostatic system of currents ::@:: For a [magnetostatic](../../../../general/magnetostatic.md) system of currents in free space, the stored energy can be found by imagining the process of linearly turning on the currents and their generated magnetic field, arriving at a total energy of: $$E={\frac {1}{2} }\int \mathbf {J} \cdot \mathbf {A} \,\mathrm {d} V$$ where $\mathbf {J}$ is the current density field and $\mathbf {A}$ is the [magnetic vector potential](../../../../general/magnetic%20vector%20potential.md). This is analogous to the [electrostatic energy](../../../../general/electrostatic%20energy.md) expression ${\frac {1}{2} }\int \rho \phi \,\mathrm {d} V$; note that neither of these static expressions apply in the case of time-varying charge or current distributions. <!--SR:!2025-09-23,117,421!2025-07-04,36,381-->

## week 10 tutorial

- datetime: 2025-04-10T18:00:00+08:00/2025-04-10T18:50:00+08:00, PT50M
- topic: midterm paper checking
- [§ midterm examination](#midterm%20examination)
- [Taylor's theorem](../../../../general/Taylor's%20theorem.md) ::@:: It gives an approximation of a $k$-times [differentiable function](../../../../general/differentiable%20function.md) around a given point by a [polynomial](../../../../general/polynomial.md) of degree $k$, called the $k$-th-order __Taylor polynomial__. <!--SR:!2025-11-06,161,441!2025-11-03,158,441-->
  - Taylor's theorem / Taylor series ::@:: For a [smooth function](../../../../general/smooth%20function.md), the Taylor polynomial is the truncation at the order $k$ of the [Taylor series](../../../../general/Taylor%20series.md) of the function. <!--SR:!2025-11-03,158,441!2025-11-08,163,440-->
  - Taylor's theorem / multivariate functions ::@:: Justified by [Clairaut's theorem](../../../../general/symmetry%20of%20second%20derivatives.md), denote the short-hand notation $$D^{\alpha }f={\frac {\partial ^{|\alpha |}f}{\partial {\boldsymbol {x} }^{\alpha } } }={\frac {\partial ^{\alpha _{1}+\ldots +\alpha _{n} }f}{\partial x_{1}^{\alpha _{1} }\cdots \partial x_{n}^{\alpha _{n} } } }$$ for the higher order [partial derivatives](../../../../general/partial%20derivatives.md). <p> Let _f_ : __R__<sup>_n_</sup> → __R__ be a _k_-times [continuously differentiable](../../../../general/continuously%20differentiable.md#continuously%20differentiable) function at the point ___a___ ∈ __R__<sup>_n_</sup>. Then there exist functions _h_<sub>_α_</sub> : __R__<sup>_n_</sup> → __R__, where $|\alpha |=k$, such that $${\begin{aligned}&f({\boldsymbol {x} })=\sum _{|\alpha |\leq k}{\frac {D^{\alpha }f({\boldsymbol {a} })}{\alpha !} }({\boldsymbol {x} }-{\boldsymbol {a} })^{\alpha }+\sum _{|\alpha |=k}h_{\alpha }({\boldsymbol {x} })({\boldsymbol {x} }-{\boldsymbol {a} })^{\alpha },\\&{\text{and} }\quad \lim _{ {\boldsymbol {x} }\to {\boldsymbol {a} } }h_{\alpha }({\boldsymbol {x} })=0.\end{aligned} }$$ <!--SR:!2025-09-20,114,421!2025-08-20,83,401-->
  - Taylor's theorem / example in two dimensions ::@:: For example, the third-order Taylor polynomial of a smooth function $f:\mathbb {R} ^{2}\to \mathbb {R}$ is, denoting ${\boldsymbol {x} }-{\boldsymbol {a} }={\boldsymbol {v} }$, $${\begin{aligned}P_{3}({\boldsymbol {x} })=f({\boldsymbol {a} })+{}&{\frac {\partial f}{\partial x_{1} } }({\boldsymbol {a} })v_{1}+{\frac {\partial f}{\partial x_{2} } }({\boldsymbol {a} })v_{2}+{\frac {\partial ^{2}f}{\partial x_{1}^{2} } }({\boldsymbol {a} }){\frac {v_{1}^{2} }{2!} }+{\frac {\partial ^{2}f}{\partial x_{1}\partial x_{2} } }({\boldsymbol {a} })v_{1}v_{2}+{\frac {\partial ^{2}f}{\partial x_{2}^{2} } }({\boldsymbol {a} }){\frac {v_{2}^{2} }{2!} }\\&+{\frac {\partial ^{3}f}{\partial x_{1}^{3} } }({\boldsymbol {a} }){\frac {v_{1}^{3} }{3!} }+{\frac {\partial ^{3}f}{\partial x_{1}^{2}\partial x_{2} } }({\boldsymbol {a} }){\frac {v_{1}^{2}v_{2} }{2!} }+{\frac {\partial ^{3}f}{\partial x_{1}\partial x_{2}^{2} } }({\boldsymbol {a} }){\frac {v_{1}v_{2}^{2} }{2!} }+{\frac {\partial ^{3}f}{\partial x_{2}^{3} } }({\boldsymbol {a} }){\frac {v_{2}^{3} }{3!} } \,. \end{aligned} }$$ <!--SR:!2025-11-05,160,440!2025-11-02,157,440-->
  - Taylor's theorem / Coulomb potential in three dimensions ::@:: $\alpha$ and $\mathbf r'$ are constants. Approximate the Coulomb potential $V(\mathbf r) = \frac {\alpha} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert}$ in three dimensions around the origin up to the third order of $\mathbf r'$ \(second order terms\). <p> The first order term of $\mathbf r'$ \(zeroth order term\) is: $$\left. V(\mathbf r) \right\rvert_{\mathbf r = \mathbf 0} = V(\mathbf 0) = \frac \alpha {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert} \,.$$ The term sums to: $$V_1(\mathbf r) = \frac 1 {0!} \frac \alpha {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert} = \frac \alpha {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert} \,.$$ <p> The second order terms of $\mathbf r'$ \(first order terms\) are its gradient: $$\left. \nabla_{\mathbf r} V(\mathbf r) \right\rvert_{\mathbf r = \mathbf 0} = \left. \frac {\alpha (\mathbf r' - \mathbf r)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^3} \right\rvert_{\mathbf r = \mathbf 0} = \frac {\alpha \mathbf r'} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^3} \,.$$ The terms sum to: $$V_2(\mathbf r) = \frac 1 {1!} \frac \alpha {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} (\hat{\mathbf r'} \cdot \mathbf r) = \frac {\alpha \lvert \mathbf r \rvert} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} (\hat{\mathbf r'} \cdot \hat{\mathbf r}) \,.$$ <p> The third order terms of $\mathbf r'$ \(third order terms\) are its Hessian matrix \($\nabla^2$ as used here is _not_ the Laplacian commonly seen elsewhere\): $$\begin{aligned} \left. \nabla_{\mathbf r}^2 V(\mathbf r) \right\rvert_{\mathbf r = \mathbf 0} & = \left. \alpha \begin{bmatrix} \frac \partial {\partial x} \\ \frac \partial {\partial y} \\ \frac \partial {\partial z} \end{bmatrix} \begin{bmatrix} \frac {r'_x - r_x} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^3} & \frac {r'_y - r_y} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^3} & \frac {r'_z - r_z} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^3} \end{bmatrix} \right\rvert_{\mathbf r = \mathbf 0} \\ & = \left. \alpha \begin{bmatrix} \frac {-1 \lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^3 - 3 (r'_x - r_x) \lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2 \frac {r_x - r'_x} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert} } {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^6} & 3\frac {r'_y - r_y} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^4} \frac {r'_x - r_x} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert} & 3\frac {r'_z - r_z} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^4} \frac {r'_x - r_x} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert} \\ 3\frac {r'_x - r_x} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^4} \frac {r'_y - r_y} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert} & \frac {-1 \lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^3 - 3 (r'_y - r_y) \lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2 \frac {r_y - r'_y} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert} } {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^6} & 3\frac {r'_z - r_z} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^4} \frac {r'_y - r_y} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert} \\ 3\frac {r'_x - r_x} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^4} \frac {r'_z - r_z} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert} & 3\frac {r'_y - r_y} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^4} \frac {r'_z - r_z} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert} & \frac {-1 \lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^3 - 3 (r'_z - r_z) \lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2 \frac {r_z - r'_z} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert} } {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^6} \end{bmatrix} \right\rvert_{\mathbf r = \mathbf 0} \\ & = \left. \frac \alpha {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^3} \begin{bmatrix} 3 \frac {(r'_x - r_x)^2} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2} - 1 & 3 \frac {(r'_x - r_x) (r'_y - r_y)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2} & 3 \frac {(r'_x - r_x) (r'_z - r_z)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2} \\ 3 \frac {(r'_x - r_x) (r'_y - r_y)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2} & 3 \frac {(r'_y - r_y)^2} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2} - 1 & 3 \frac {(r'_y - r_y)(r'_z - r_z)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2} \\ 3 \frac {(r'_x - r_x) (r'_z - r_z)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2} & 3 \frac {(r'_y - r_y)(r'_z - r_z)} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2} & 3 \frac {(r'_z - r_z)^2} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert^2} - 1 \end{bmatrix} \right\rvert_{\mathbf r = \mathbf 0} \\ & = \frac \alpha {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^3} \begin{bmatrix} 3 \frac {(r'_x)^2} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} - 1 & 3 \frac {r'_x r'_y} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} & 3 \frac {r'_x r'_z} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} \\ 3 \frac {r'_x r'_y} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} & 3 \frac {(r'_y)^2} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} - 1 & 3 \frac {r'_y r'_z} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} \\ 3 \frac {r'_x r'_z} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} & 3 \frac {r'_y r'_z} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} & 3 \frac {(r'_z)^2} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} - 1 \end{bmatrix} \,. \end{aligned}$$ The terms sum to: $$V_3(\mathbf r) = \frac 1 {2!} \frac {\alpha} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^3} \left(3 (\hat{\mathbf r'} \cdot \mathbf r )^2 - \lvert \mathbf r \rvert^2 \right) =  \frac {\alpha \lvert \mathbf r \rvert^2} {2 \lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^3} \left(3 (\hat{\mathbf r'} \cdot \hat{\mathbf r} )^2 - 1 \right)$$ <p> Finally, the approximation is: $$V(\mathbf r) \approx \frac \alpha {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert} + \frac {\alpha \lvert \mathbf r \rvert} {\lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^2} (\hat{\mathbf r'} \cdot \hat{\mathbf r}) + \frac {\alpha \lvert \mathbf r \rvert^2} {2 \lvert \mathbf r' \rvert^3} \left(3 (\hat{\mathbf r'} \cdot \hat{\mathbf r})^2 - 1 \right) \,.$$ <p> If you want a much less messy but less intuitive \(at first\) derivation, use _multipole expansion_. <!--SR:!2025-07-24,77,401!2025-07-10,64,381-->
- [multipole expansion](../../../../general/multipole%20expansion.md) ::@:: It is a [mathematical series](../../../../general/series%20(mathematics).md) representing a [function](../../../../general/function%20(mathematics).md) that depends on [angles](../../../../general/angle.md)—usually the two angles used in the [spherical coordinate system](../../../../general/spherical%20coordinate%20system.md) \(the polar and [azimuthal](../../../../general/azimuth.md) angles\) for three-dimensional [Euclidean space](../../../../general/Euclidean%20space.md), $\mathbb {R} ^{3}$. Multipole expansions are useful because, similar to [Taylor series](../../../../general/Taylor%20series.md), oftentimes only the first few terms are needed to provide a good approximation of the original function. The function being expanded may be [real](../../../../general/real%20number.md)- or [complex](../../../../general/complex%20number.md)-valued and is defined either on $\mathbb {R} ^{3}$, or less often on $\mathbb {R} ^{n}$ for some other $n$. <!--SR:!2025-11-07,162,441!2025-11-07,162,440-->
  - multipole expansion / Coulomb potential in three dimensions ::@:: $\alpha$ and $\mathbf r'$ are constants. Approximate the Coulomb potential $V(\mathbf r) = \frac {\alpha} {\lvert \mathbf r - \mathbf r' \rvert}$ in three dimensions around the origin up to the third order of $\mathbf r'$ \(second order terms\). <p> Rewrite the potential in terms of $r = \lvert \mathbf r \rvert$, $r' = \lvert \mathbf r' \rvert$, and $\theta$ \(the angle between $\mathbf r$ and $\mathbf r'$\): $$\begin{aligned} V(\mathbf r) & = \frac {\alpha} {\sqrt{r^2 + (r')^2 - 2 r r' \cos \theta} } \\ & = \frac \alpha {r'} \frac 1 {\sqrt{1 + (r / r')^2 - 2(r / r') \cos \theta} } \\ & = \frac {\alpha} {r'} \frac 1 {\sqrt{1 + (r / r')(r / r' - 2 \cos\theta)} } \,. \end{aligned}$$ Apply Taylor expansion up to the second order term: $$\begin{aligned} V(\mathbf r) & \approx \frac \alpha {r'} \left(\binom {-1/2} 0 \right. \\ & \left. + \binom {-1/2} 1 (r / r') (r / r' - 2 \cos \theta) \right. \\ & \left. + \binom {-1/2} 2 (r / r')^2 (r / r' - 2 \cos \theta)^2 \right) \\ & = \frac \alpha {r'} \left(1\right. \\ & \left. -\frac 1 2 (r / r') (r / r' - 2 \cos \theta) \right. \\ & \left. +\frac 3 8 (r / r')^2 (r / r' - 2 \cos \theta)^2 \right) \,. \end{aligned}$$ Collect terms: $$\begin{aligned} V(\mathbf r) & \approx \frac \alpha {r'} \\ & + \frac {\alpha r} {(r')^2} \left( \left(-\frac 1 2 \right)(-2 \cos \theta) \right) \\ & + \frac {\alpha r^2} {(r')^3} \left(\left(-\frac 1 2\right) + \frac 3 8 \left(4 \cos^2 \theta\right) \right) \\ & \approx \frac {\alpha} r + \frac {\alpha r} {(r')^2} \cos \theta + \frac {\alpha r^2} {2 (r')^3} \left(3 \cos^2 \theta - 1 \right) \,. \end{aligned}$$ <p> Compare with _Taylor's theorem_. <!--SR:!2025-07-26,80,401!2025-09-26,120,421-->

## week 11 lecture

- datetime: 2025-04-14T09:00:00+08:00/2025-04-14T10:20:00+08:00, PT1H20M
- topic: dynamics of electric and magnetic energy, LCR circuits
- [LC circuit](../../../../general/LC%20circuit.md) ::@:: It is an electric circuit consisting of an inductor, represented by the letter L, and a capacitor, represented by the letter C, connected together. The circuit can act as an electrical resonator, an electrical analogue of a tuning fork, storing energy oscillating at the circuit's resonant frequency. <!--SR:!2025-12-07,164,448!2025-12-01,159,448-->
- [RL circuit](../../../../general/RL%20circuit.md) ::@:: It is an electric circuit composed of resistors and inductors driven by a voltage or current source. <!--SR:!2025-06-27,29,428!2025-12-03,160,450-->
  - RL circuit / current growth ::@:: Assume the voltage $v$ is a step function: $$v = \begin{cases} v_0 & t \ge 0 \\ 0 & \text{otherwise} \,. \end{cases}$$ We have: $$v_0 = Ri + L \frac{\mathrm di} {\mathrm dt} \qquad t \ge 0 \,.$$ Solve: $$\begin{aligned} v_0 & = Ri + L \frac {\mathrm di} {\mathrm dt} \\ \frac {\mathrm di} {\mathrm dt} & = \frac {v_0 - Ri} L \\ \mathrm di \, \frac 1 {v_0 / R - i} & = \mathrm dt \, \frac R L \\ \int \! \mathrm di \, \frac 1 {v_0 / R - i} & = \int \! \mathrm dt \, \frac R L \\ \ln(v_0 / R - i) & = -\frac R L t + c_1 && v_0 / R > i \\ \frac {v_0} R - i & = e^{c_1 - \frac R L t} \\ i & = \frac {v_0} R - e^{c_1 - \frac R L t} \\ & = \frac {v_0} R \left(1 - e^{-\frac R L t}\right) && i(0) = 0 \implies e^{c_1} = \frac {v_0} R \,. \end{aligned}$$ <!--SR:!2025-12-05,162,450!2025-12-05,162,450-->
    - RL circuit / current growth / dynamics ::@:: current: $i = \frac {v_0} R \left(1 - e^{- \frac R L t} \right)$ <br/> current derivative: $\frac {\mathrm di} {\mathrm dt} = \frac {v_0} L e^{-\frac R L t}$ <!--SR:!2025-06-27,29,430!2025-12-06,163,448-->
    - RL circuit / current growth / parameters ::@:: current at $t = 0$: $i_0 = 0$ <br/> current at $t = \infty$: $i_\infty = v_0 / R$ <br/> decay constant: $\lambda = R / L = 1 / \tau$, in radians per second <br/> time constant: $\tau = L / R = 1 / \lambda$, the time it takes for the current difference from $i_\infty$ to be multiplied by $\frac 1 e$ <!--SR:!2025-10-12,109,428!2025-12-06,163,450-->
    - RL circuit / current growth / interpretation ::@:: Current grows from 0 to $i_\infty = v_0 / R$ exponentially slowly with a decay constant of $\lambda = R / L$. Higher $R$ hasten the growth, while higher $L$ slows the growth. <!--SR:!2025-06-27,29,428!2025-12-10,167,450-->
  - RL circuit / current decay ::@:: Assume the voltage $v$ is a step function: $$v = \begin{cases} 0 & t \ge 0 \\ v_0 & \text{otherwise} \,. \end{cases}$$ We have: $$0 = Ri + L \frac{\mathrm di} {\mathrm dt} \qquad t \ge 0 \,.$$ Solve: $$\begin{aligned} 0 & = Ri + L \frac {\mathrm di} {\mathrm dt} \\ \frac {\mathrm di} {\mathrm dt} & = -\frac {Ri} L \\ \mathrm di \, \frac 1 i & = -\mathrm dt \, \frac R L \\ \int \! \mathrm di \, \frac 1 i & = -\int \! \mathrm dt \, \frac R L \\ \ln i & = -\frac R L t + c_1 && i > 0 \\ i & = e^{c_1 - \frac R L t} \\ & = \frac {v_0} R e^{-\frac R L t} && i(0) = \frac {v_0} R \implies e^{c_1} = \frac {v_0} R \,. \end{aligned}$$ <!--SR:!2025-09-01,67,388!2025-06-27,29,430-->
    - RL circuit / current decay / dynamics ::@:: current: $i = \frac {v_0} R e^{- \frac R L t}$ <br/> current derivative: $\frac {\mathrm di} {\mathrm dt} = -\frac {v_0} L e^{-\frac R L t}$ <!--SR:!2025-10-18,114,428!2025-12-07,164,450-->
    - RL circuit / current decay / parameters ::@:: current at $t = 0$: $i_0 = v_0 / R$ <br/> current at $t = \infty$: $i_\infty = 0$ <br/> decay constant: $\lambda = R / L = 1 / \tau$, in radians per second <br/> time constant: $\tau = L / R = 1 / \lambda$, the time it takes for the current difference from $t_\infty$ to be multiplied by $\frac 1 e$ <!--SR:!2025-12-02,160,450!2025-10-17,113,430-->
    - RL circuit / current decay / interpretation ::@:: Current decays from $i_\infty = v_0 / R$ to 0 exponentially slowly with a decay constant of $\lambda = R / L$. Higher $R$ hasten the decay, while higher $L$ slows the decay. <!--SR:!2025-12-01,158,450!2025-11-28,155,449-->
  - RL circuit / energy ::@:: The inductor stores energy, up to a maximum of $L i^2 / 2$. The resistor dissipates energy, at a rate of $R i^2$. <!--SR:!2025-11-28,156,448!2025-11-27,155,450-->
- LC circuit
  - LC circuit / operation ::@:: If an inductor is connected across a charged capacitor, the voltage across the capacitor will drive a current through the inductor, building up a magnetic field around it. The voltage across the capacitor falls to zero as the charge is used up by the current flow. At this point, the energy stored in the coil's magnetic field induces a voltage across the coil, because inductors oppose changes in current. This induced voltage causes a current to begin to recharge the capacitor with a voltage of opposite polarity to its original charge. Due to Faraday's law, the EMF which drives the current is caused by a decrease in the magnetic field, thus the energy required to charge the capacitor is extracted from the magnetic field. When the magnetic field is completely dissipated the current will stop and the charge will again be stored in the capacitor, with the opposite polarity as before. Then the cycle will begin again, with the current flowing in the opposite direction through the inductor. <!--SR:!2025-12-05,162,448!2025-11-30,157,448-->
  - LC circuit / time domain solution ::@:: A time domain solution for the LC circuit can be obtained using Kirchhoff's laws to form a differential equation, solve it, and plug in the initial conditions. <!--SR:!2025-06-27,29,430!2025-06-27,29,430-->
    - LC circuit / time domain solution / Kirchhoff's laws ::@:: By [Kirchhoff's voltage law](../../../../general/Kirchhoff's%20voltage%20law.md#Kirchhoff's%20voltage%20law), the voltage _V<sub>C</sub>_ across the capacitor plus the voltage _V<sub>L</sub>_ across the inductor must equal zero: $$V_{C}+V_{L}=0.$$ Likewise, by [Kirchhoff's current law](../../../../general/Kirchhoff's%20current%20law.md#Kirchhoff's%20current%20law%20(KCL)), the current through the capacitor equals the current through the inductor: $$I_{C}=I_{L}.$$ From the constitutive relations for the circuit elements, we also know that $${\begin{aligned}V_{L}(t)&=L{\frac {\mathrm {d} I_{L} }{\mathrm {d} t} },\\I_{C}(t)&=C{\frac {\mathrm {d} V_{C} }{\mathrm {d} t} }.\end{aligned} }$$ \(annotation: Note that voltages above are voltage _drops_.\) <!--SR:!2025-12-06,163,450!2025-09-27,93,410-->
    - LC circuit / time domain solution / differential equation ::@:: Rearranging and substituting gives the second order [differential equation](../../../../general/differential%20equation.md) $${\frac {\mathrm {d} ^{2} }{\mathrm {d} t^{2} } }I(t)+{\frac {1}{LC} }I(t)=0.$$ The parameter _ω_<sub>0</sub>, the resonant [angular frequency](../../../../general/angular%20frequency.md), is defined as $$\omega _{0}={\frac {1}{\sqrt {LC} } }.$$ Using this can simplify the differential equation: $${\frac {\mathrm {d} ^{2} }{\mathrm {d} t^{2} } }I(t)+\omega _{0}^{2}I(t)=0.$$ The associated [Laplace transform](../../../../general/Laplace%20transform.md) is $$s^{2}+\omega _{0}^{2}=0,$$ thus $$s=\pm j\omega _{0},$$ where _j_ is the [imaginary unit](../../../../general/imaginary%20unit.md). <!--SR:!2025-10-29,125,430!2025-11-27,154,448-->
    - LC circuit / time domain solution / solution ::@:: Thus, the complete solution to the differential equation is $$I(t)=Ae^{+j\omega _{0}t}+Be^{-j\omega _{0}t}$$ and can be solved for _A_ and _B_ by considering the initial conditions. Since the exponential is [complex](../../../../general/complex%20numbers.md), the solution represents a sinusoidal [alternating current](../../../../general/alternating%20current.md). Since the electric current _I_ is a physical quantity, it must be real-valued. As a result, it can be shown that the constants _A_ and _B_ must be [complex conjugates](../../../../general/complex%20conjugate.md): $$A=B^{*}.$$ Now let $$A={\frac {I_{0} }{2} }e^{+j\phi }.$$ Therefore, $$B={\frac {I_{0} }{2} }e^{-j\phi }.$$ Next, we can use [Euler's formula](../../../../general/Euler's%20formula.md) to obtain a real [sinusoid](../../../../general/sine%20wave.md) with [amplitude](../../../../general/amplitude.md) _I_<sub>0</sub>, [angular frequency](../../../../general/angular%20frequency.md) _ω_<sub>0</sub> = ⁠1/√\(_LC_\)⁠, and [phase angle](../../../../general/phase%20(waves).md) $\phi$. Thus, the resulting solution becomes $$I(t)=I_{0}\cos \left(\omega _{0}t+\phi \right),$$ <br/> $$V_{L}(t)=L{\frac {\mathrm {d} I}{\mathrm {d} t} }=-\omega _{0}LI_{0}\sin \left(\omega _{0}t+\phi \right).$$ <!--SR:!2025-09-26,92,410!2025-08-28,64,390-->
    - LC circuit / time domain solution / initial conditions ::@:: The initial conditions that would satisfy this result are $$I(0)=I_{0}\cos \phi ,$$ <br/> $$V_{L}(0)=L{\frac {\mathrm {d} I}{\mathrm {d} t} }{\Bigg |}_{t=0}=-\omega _{0}LI_{0}\sin \phi .$$ <!--SR:!2025-10-20,116,428!2025-09-20,86,408-->
  - LC circuit / energy ::@:: An LC circuit, oscillating at its natural resonant frequency, can store electrical energy. <!-- See the animation. --> A capacitor stores energy in the electric field \ (_E_\) between its plates, depending on the voltage across it, and an inductor stores energy in its magnetic field \(_B_\), depending on the current through it. <p> The charge flows back and forth between the plates of the capacitor, through the inductor. The energy oscillates back and forth between the capacitor and the inductor until \(if not replenished from an external circuit\) internal resistance makes the oscillations die out. <!--SR:!2025-10-25,122,430!2025-10-17,114,430-->
  - LC circuit / assumptions ::@:: An LC circuit is an idealized model since it assumes there is no dissipation of energy due to resistance. Any practical implementation of an LC circuit will always include loss resulting from small but non-zero resistance within the components and connecting wires. <!--SR:!2025-11-29,156,450!2025-06-27,29,430-->
- [RLC circuit](../../../../general/RLC%20circuit.md) ::@:: It is an electrical circuit consisting of a resistor \(R\), an inductor \(L\), and a capacitor \(C\), connected in series or in parallel. The name of the circuit is derived from the letters that are used to denote the constituent components of this circuit, where the sequence of the components may vary from RLC. <!--SR:!2025-12-08,165,450!2025-06-27,29,430-->
  - RLC circuit / series circuit ::@:: In this circuit, the three components are all in series with the voltage source. <p>  A time domain solution for the RLC circuit can be obtained using Kirchhoff's laws to form a differential equation, solve it, and plug in the initial conditions. <!--SR:!2025-12-09,166,450!2025-12-10,167,450-->
    - RLC circuit / series circuit / Kirchhoff's laws ::@:: The governing [differential equation](../../../../general/differential%20equation.md) can be found by substituting into [Kirchhoff's voltage law](../../../../general/Kirchhoff's%20voltage%20law.md#Kirchhoff's%20voltage%20law) \(KVL\) the [constitutive equation](../../../../general/constitutive%20equation.md) for each of the three elements. From the KVL, $$V_{\mathrm {R} }+V_{\mathrm {L} }+V_{\mathrm {C} }=V(t)\,,$$ where _V<sub>R</sub>_, _V_<sub>L</sub> and _V_<sub>C</sub> are the voltages \(annotation: _voltage drops_\) across _R_, _L_, and _C_, respectively, and _V_\(_t_\) is the time-varying voltage from the source. Substituting $V_{R}=R\ I(t)\,$, $\,V_{\mathrm {L} }=L{\frac {\mathrm {d} I(t)}{\mathrm {d} t} }\,$ and $\,V_{\mathrm {C} }=V(0)+{\frac {1}{\,C\,} }\int _{0}^{t}I(\tau )\,\mathrm {d} \tau \,$ into the equation above yields: $$RI(t)+L{\frac {\,\mathrm {d} I(t)\,}{\mathrm {d} t} }+V(0)+{\frac {1}{\,C\,} }\int _{0}^{t}I(\tau )\,\mathrm {d} \tau =V(t)\;.$$ <!--SR:!2025-09-24,90,410!2025-06-27,29,430-->
    - RLC circuit / series circuit / differential equation ::@:: For the case where the source is an unchanging voltage, taking the time derivative and dividing by _L_ leads to the following second order differential equation: $${\frac {\mathrm {d} ^{2} }{\mathrm {d} t^{2} } }I(t)+{\frac {R}{\,L\,} }{\frac {\mathrm {d} }{\mathrm {d} t} }I(t)+{\frac {1}{\,L\,C\,} }I(t)=0\;.$$ This can usefully be expressed in a more generally applicable form: $${\frac {\mathrm {d} ^{2} }{\mathrm {d} t^{2} } }I(t)+2\alpha {\frac {\mathrm {d} }{\mathrm {d} t} }I(t)+\omega _{0}^{2}I(t)=0\;.$$ _α_ and _ω_<sub>0</sub> are both in units of [angular frequency](../../../../general/angular%20frequency.md). _α_ is called the _neper frequency_, or _attenuation_, and is a measure of how fast the [transient response](../../../../general/transient%20response.md) of the circuit will die away after the stimulus has been removed. Neper occurs in the name because the units can also be considered to be [nepers](../../../../general/neper.md) per second, neper being a [logarithmic unit](../../../../general/logarithmic%20unit.md#logarithmic%20units) of attenuation. _ω_<sub>0</sub> is the angular resonance frequency. <!--SR:!2025-06-27,29,430!2025-06-27,29,430-->
    - RLC circuit / series circuit / parameters ::@:: _α_ and _ω_<sub>0</sub> are both in units of [angular frequency](../../../../general/angular%20frequency.md). _α_ is called the _neper frequency_, or _attenuation_, and is a measure of how fast the [transient response](../../../../general/transient%20response.md) of the circuit will die away after the stimulus has been removed. Neper occurs in the name because the units can also be considered to be [nepers](../../../../general/neper.md) per second, neper being a [logarithmic unit](../../../../general/logarithmic%20unit.md#logarithmic%20units) of attenuation. _ω_<sub>0</sub> is the angular resonance frequency. <p> For the case of the series RLC circuit these two parameters are given by: $${\begin{aligned}\alpha &={\frac {R}{\,2L\,} }\\\omega _{0}&={\frac {1}{\,{\sqrt {L\,C\,} }\,} }\;.\end{aligned} }$$ <p> A useful parameter is the _damping factor_, _ζ_, which is defined as the ratio of these two; although, sometimes _ζ_ is not used, and _α_ is referred to as _damping factor_ instead; hence requiring careful specification of one's use of that term. $$\zeta \equiv {\frac {\alpha }{\,\omega _{0}\,} }\;.$$ <p> In the case of the series RLC circuit, the damping factor is given by $$\zeta ={\frac {\,R\,}{2} }{\sqrt { {\frac {C}{\,L\,} }\,} }={\frac {1}{\ 2Q\ } }~.$$ The value of the damping factor determines the type of transient that the circuit will exhibit. <!--SR:!2025-09-10,76,390!2025-09-28,94,410-->
    - RLC circuit / series circuit / transient response ::@:: The differential equation has the [characteristic equation](../../../../general/linear%20homogeneous%20differential%20equation.md#homogeneous%20equation%20with%20constant%20coefficients), $$s^{2}+2\alpha s+\omega _{0}^{2}=0\,.$$ The roots of the equation in _s_-domain are, $${\begin{aligned}s_{1}&=-\alpha +{\sqrt {\alpha ^{2}-\omega _{0}^{2}\,} }=-\omega _{0}\left(\zeta -{\sqrt {\zeta ^{2}-1\,} }\right)={\frac {-\omega _{0} }{\ \zeta +{\sqrt {\zeta ^{2}-1\ } }\ } }\ ,\\s_{2}&=-\alpha -{\sqrt {\alpha ^{2}-\omega _{0}^{2}\,} }=-\omega _{0}\left(\zeta +{\sqrt {\zeta ^{2}-1\,} }\right)\;.\end{aligned} }$$ The general solution of the differential equation is an exponential in either root or a linear superposition of both, $$I(t)=A_{1}e^{s_{1}t}+A_{2}e^{s_{2}t}\;.$$ The coefficients _A_<sub>1</sub> and _A_<sub>2</sub> are determined by the [boundary conditions](../../../../general/boundary%20condition.md) of the specific problem being analysed. That is, they are set by the values of the currents and voltages in the circuit at the onset of the transient and the presumed value they will settle to after infinite time. <!--SR:!2025-09-02,68,390!2025-09-16,82,410-->
    - RLC circuit / series circuit / underdamped response ::@:: The underdamped response \(_ζ_ \< 1\) is $$I(t)=B_{1}e^{-\alpha t}\cos(\omega _{\mathrm {d} }t)+B_{2}e^{-\alpha t}\sin(\omega _{\mathrm {d} }t)\,.$$ By applying standard [trigonometric identities](../../../../general/list%20of%20trigonometric%20identities.md#linear%20combinations) the two trigonometric functions may be expressed as a single sinusoid with phase shift, $$I(t)=B_{3}e^{-\alpha t}\sin(\omega _{\mathrm {d} }t+\varphi )\;.$$ The underdamped response is a decaying oscillation at frequency _ω_<sub>d</sub>. The oscillation decays at a rate determined by the attenuation α. The exponential in α describes the [envelope](../../../../general/envelope%20(waves).md) of the oscillation. _B_<sub>1</sub> and _B_<sub>2</sub> \(or _B_<sub>3</sub> and the phase shift _φ_ in the second form\) are arbitrary constants determined by boundary conditions. The frequency _ω_<sub>d</sub> is given by $$\omega _{\mathrm {d} }={\sqrt {\omega _{0}^{2}-\alpha ^{2} } }=\omega _{0}{\sqrt {1-\zeta ^{2}\,} } = \sqrt{\frac 1 {LC} - \frac {R^2} {4L^2} } \;.$$ This is called the damped resonance frequency or the damped natural frequency. It is the frequency the circuit will naturally oscillate at if not driven by an external source. The resonance frequency, _ω_<sub>0</sub>, which is the frequency at which the circuit will resonate when driven by an external oscillation, may often be referred to as the undamped resonance frequency to distinguish it. <!--SR:!2025-07-03,7,348!2025-08-29,64,390-->
    - RLC circuit / series circuit / critically damped response ::@:: The critically damped response \(_ζ_ = 1\) is $$I(t)=D_{1}te^{-\alpha t}+D_{2}e^{-\alpha t}\;.$$ The critically damped response represents the circuit response that decays in the fastest possible time without going into oscillation. This consideration is important in control systems where it is required to reach the desired state as quickly as possible without overshooting. _D_<sub>1</sub> and _D_<sub>2</sub> are arbitrary constants determined by boundary conditions. <!--SR:!2025-10-23,119,428!2025-10-30,126,430-->
    - RLC circuit / series circuit / overdamped response ::@:: The overdamped response \(_ζ_ \> 1\) is $$I(t)=A_{1}e^{-\omega _{0}\left(\zeta +{\sqrt {\zeta ^{2}-1} }\right)t}+A_{2}e^{-\omega _{0}\left(\zeta -{\sqrt {\zeta ^{2}-1} }\right)t}\;.$$ The overdamped response is a decay of the transient current without oscillation. <!--SR:!2025-09-28,94,410!2025-09-07,74,388-->
- extras
  - [simple harmonic motion](../../../../general/simple%20harmonic%20motion.md) ::@:: It is a special type of periodic motion an object experiences by means of a restoring force whose magnitude is directly proportional to the distance of the object from an equilibrium position and acts towards the equilibrium position. It results in an oscillation that is described by a sinusoid which continues indefinitely \(if uninhibited by friction or any other dissipation of energy\). <!--SR:!2025-11-30,157,450!2025-12-06,163,448-->
  - [harmonic oscillator](../../../../general/harmonic%20oscillator.md) ::@:: It is is a system that, when displaced from its equilibrium position, experiences a restoring force _F_ proportional to the displacement _x_: $${\vec {F}}=-k{\vec {x}},$$ where _k_ is a positive constant. <!--SR:!2025-06-27,29,430!2025-12-09,166,448-->
  - [damping](../../../../general/damping.md) ::@:: It is the loss of energy of an oscillating system by dissipation. Damping is an influence within or upon an oscillatory system that has the effect of reducing or preventing its oscillation. <!--SR:!2025-12-02,159,450!2025-06-27,29,428-->

## week 11 lecture 2

- datetime: 2025-04-16T09:00:00+08:00/2025-04-16T10:20:00+08:00, PT1H20M
- topic: electromagnetic waves
- [electromagnetic radiation](../../../../general/electromagnetic%20radiation.md) \(EMR\) ::@:: It is a self-propagating wave of the electromagnetic field that carries momentum and radiant energy through space. <!--SR:!2025-12-04,161,450!2025-06-27,29,430-->
- [Heinrich Hertz](../../../../general/Heinrich%20Hertz.md) \(1857–1894\) ::@:: He was a German physicist who first conclusively proved the existence of the electromagnetic waves predicted by James Clerk Maxwell's equations of electromagnetism. <!--SR:!2025-12-01,159,450!2025-06-27,29,428-->
  - Heinrich Hertz / electromagnetic waves experiment ::@:: He used a dipole antenna consisting of two collinear one-meter wires with a spark gap between their inner ends, and zinc spheres attached to the outer ends for capacitance, as a radiator. The antenna was excited by pulses of high voltage of about 30 kilovolts applied between the two sides from a Ruhmkorff coil. He received the waves with a resonant single-loop antenna with a micrometer spark gap between the ends. This experiment produced and received what are now called radio waves in the very high frequency range. <!--SR:!2025-12-08,165,450!2025-10-13,109,430-->
    - Heinrich Hertz / electromagnetic waves experiment / observations ::@:: Using two wires with a spark gap in between them as the receiver, and connecting a light bulb between them instead. <p> When the emitter is sparking, the light bulb lights up briefly. The distance and relative orientation of the receiver affect the lamp intensity. <p> This proves the existence of electromagnetic waves. Their emission has a distinct spatial pattern. Their amplitudes decay with distance. <!--SR:!2025-12-04,161,448!2025-12-08,165,450-->
- [wave equation](../../../../general/wave%20equation.md) ::@:: It is a second-order linear partial differential equation for the description of waves or standing wave fields such as mechanical waves (e.g. water waves, sound waves and seismic waves) or electromagnetic waves \(including light waves\). It arises in fields like acoustics, electromagnetism, and fluid dynamics. <!--SR:!2025-06-27,29,430!2025-12-05,162,448-->
  - wave equation / scalar wave equation ::@:: The scalar wave equation is $${\frac {\partial ^{2}u}{\partial t^{2} } }=c^{2}\left({\frac {\partial ^{2}u}{\partial x^{2} } }+{\frac {\partial ^{2}u}{\partial y^{2} } }+{\frac {\partial ^{2}u}{\partial z^{2} } }\right)$$ where <p> - _c_ is a fixed non-negative [real](../../../../general/real%20number.md) [coefficient](../../../../general/coefficient.md) representing the [propagation speed](../../../../general/wave.md#wave%20velocity) of the wave <br/> - _u_ is a [scalar field](../../../../general/scalar%20field.md) representing the displacement or, more generally, the conserved quantity \(e.g. [pressure](../../../../general/pressure.md) or [density](../../../../general/density.md)\) <br/> - _x_, _y_ and _z_ are the three spatial coordinates and _t_ being the time coordinate. <!--SR:!2025-06-27,29,430!2025-12-08,165,450-->
  - wave equation / string ::@:: Consider a string with linear mass density $\mu$ and tension $T$. The net force experienced by an infinitesimal segment of the string from $x$ to $x + \Delta x$ is: $$F = T(-\sin\theta + \sin\theta') \approx T(-\tan\theta + \tan\theta') \,,$$ where $\theta$ is the counterclockwise angle from the $x$-axis at $x$ and $\theta'$ is that at $x + \Delta x$, and positive $F$ is upwards. The rightmost equation uses small-angle approximation. $\tan\theta$ can be identified as the slope at $x$, and same for $\tan\theta'$. So we have: $$T\left(-\frac {\partial f}{\partial x}(x) + \frac {\partial f}{\partial x}(x + \Delta x) \right) \approx T \Delta x \frac{\partial^2 f} {\partial x^2}(x) \,.$$ Finally, by Newton's first law: $$\mu \Delta x \frac {\partial^2 f} {\partial t^2} = T \Delta x \frac {\partial^2 f} {\partial x^2} \implies \frac {\partial^2 f} {\partial t^2} = \frac T \mu \frac {\partial^2 f} {\partial x^2} \,,$$ so we can identify the wave speed with $$c = \sqrt{\frac T \mu} \,.$$ <!--SR:!2025-09-27,93,410!2025-12-02,159,450-->
  - wave equation / general solution ::@:: For the one-dimensional wave equation a relatively simple general solution may be found. Defining new variables $${\begin{aligned}\xi &=x-ct,\\\eta &=x+ct\end{aligned} }$$ changes the wave equation into $${\frac {\partial ^{2}u}{\partial \xi \partial \eta } }(x,t)=0,$$which leads to the general solution $$u(x,t)=F(\xi )+G(\eta )=F(x-ct)+G(x+ct).$$ In other words, the solution is the sum of a right-traveling function _F_ and a left-traveling function _G_. "Traveling" means that the shape of these individual arbitrary functions with respect to _x_ stays constant, however, the functions are translated left and right with time at the speed _c_. This was derived by [Jean le Rond d'Alembert](../../../../general/Jean%20le%20Rond%20d'Alembert.md). <!--SR:!2025-11-27,154,448!2025-06-27,29,430-->
- [sine wave](../../../../general/sine%20wave.md) ::@:: It is a periodic wave whose waveform \(shape\) is the trigonometric sine function. <!--SR:!2025-11-29,157,448!2025-06-27,29,428-->
  - sine wave / sinusoid form ::@:: Sine waves of arbitrary phase and amplitude are called _sinusoids_ and have the general form: $$y(t)=A\sin(\omega t+\varphi )=A\sin(2\pi ft+\varphi )$$ where: <p> - $A$, _[amplitude](../../../../general/amplitude.md)_, the peak deviation of the function from zero. <br/> - $t$, the [real](../../../../general/real%20number.md) [independent variable](../../../../general/independent%20variable.md), usually representing [time](../../../../general/time.md) in [seconds](../../../../general/seconds.md). <br/> - $\omega$, _[angular frequency](../../../../general/angular%20frequency.md)_, the rate of change of the function argument in units of [radians per second](../../../../general/radians%20per%20second.md). <br/> - $f$, _[ordinary frequency](../../../../general/ordinary%20frequency.md)_, the _[number](../../../../general/real%20number.md)_ of oscillations \([cycles](../../../../general/turn%20(angle).md)\) that occur each second of time. <br/> - $\varphi$, _[phase](../../../../general/phase%20(waves).md)_, specifies \(in [radians](../../../../general/radian.md)\) where in its cycle the oscillation is at _t_ = 0. <!--SR:!2025-12-03,161,450!2025-10-27,124,430-->
  - sine wave / function of both position and time ::@:: Sinusoids that exist in both position and time also have: <p> - a spatial variable $x$ that represents the _position_ on the dimension on which the wave propagates. <br/> - a [wave number](../../../../general/wave%20number.md) \(or angular wave number\) $k$, which represents the proportionality between the [angular frequency](../../../../general/angular%20frequency.md) $\omega$ and the linear speed \([speed of propagation](../../../../general/phase%20velocity.md)\) $v$: <br/> &emsp; - wavenumber is related to the angular frequency by $k{=}{\frac {\omega }{v} }{=}{\frac {2\pi f}{v} }{=}{\frac {2\pi }{\lambda } }$ where $\lambda$ \([lambda](../../../../general/lambda.md)\) is the [wavelength](../../../../general/wavelength.md). <p> Depending on their direction of travel, they can take the form: <p> - $y(x,t)=A\sin(kx-\omega t+\varphi )$, if the wave is moving to the right, or <br/> - $y(x,t)=A\sin(kx+\omega t+\varphi )$, if the wave is moving to the left. <!--SR:!2025-12-05,162,448!2025-12-08,165,450-->
  - sine wave / Euler's formula ::@:: Using Euler's formula $$e^{i\theta} = \cos \theta + i \sin\theta \,,$$ we can rewrite the above function as $$y(x, t) = \mathcal R\{A e^{i(kx - \omega t + \varphi)}\}\,.$$ This is related to the _complex_ wave function $$\tilde y(x, t) = A e^{i \varphi} e^{i(kx - \omega t)} = \tilde A e^{i(kx - \omega t)} \,,$$ where $\tilde A = A e^{i }\varphi$. Its real part recovers the sine/cosine wave function. <!--SR:!2025-06-27,29,430!2025-12-05,162,448-->
